-- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
-- General-purpose LLM query — proxy any prompt to any adapter/model.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 prompt="Explain quantum computing in one sentence"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 prompt="Write a haiku about coding"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m mistral prompt="What is 2+2?"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter anthropic prompt="Summarise the history of AI"
--
-- Parameters:
--   prompt  (required)  The user prompt / question

PROMPT ollama_proxy
SELECT
    system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
    context.prompt AS prompt
GENERATE answer(prompt)
```

-- Recipe 03: Multilingual Greeting
-- Greet in any language — demonstrates parametric context with lang.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Chinese"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="French"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Japanese"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Spanish"

PROMPT multilingual_greeting
SELECT
    system_role('You are a friendly assistant. Respond in the language specified.'),
    context.user_input AS input,
    context.lang AS lang
GENERATE greeting(input, lang)

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 04: Model Showdown
-- Same prompt to multiple Ollama models — compare responses side-by-side.
-- Uses parallel CTEs instead of a bash loop for a pure-SPL implementation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="What is the meaning of life?"
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="Explain recursion in 3 sentences" \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=llama3.2 model_3=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION compare_responses(
    prompt TEXT,
    model_1 TEXT, answer_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT, answer_2 TEXT,
    model_3 TEXT, answer_3 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a neutral evaluator comparing responses from three AI models.

Prompt given to all models: "{prompt}"

First, reproduce each model's raw response exactly as given, formatted like this:

=== {model_1} ===
{answer_1}

=== {model_2} ===
{answer_2}

=== {model_3} ===
{answer_3}

Then provide your evaluation. For each model briefly assess:
- Response quality
- Key strengths or weaknesses

Conclude with which model gave the most helpful answer and why.
$$;

WORKFLOW model_showdown
    INPUT:
        @prompt  TEXT DEFAULT 'What is the meaning of life?',
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'phi4',
        @model_3 TEXT DEFAULT 'qwen2.5'
    OUTPUT: @comparison TEXT
DO
    -- Run the same prompt against all three models in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        response_1 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_1
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        response_2 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_2
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),
        response_3 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_3
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_3
        )
    SELECT
        response_1.answer AS answer_1,
        response_2.answer AS answer_2,
        response_3.answer AS answer_3
    INTO @answer_1, @answer_2, @answer_3

    -- Judge all three responses together
    GENERATE compare_responses(
        @prompt,
        @model_1, @answer_1,
        @model_2, @answer_2,
        @model_3, @answer_3
    ) INTO @comparison

    COMMIT @comparison WITH
        status   = 'complete',
        model_1  = @model_1,
        model_2  = @model_2,
        model_3  = @model_3

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'One or more models failed to respond.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

[05] Self-Refine

```

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Self-Refine Pattern
-- Iteratively improves output through critique and refinement

WORKFLOW self_refine
  INPUT: 
    @task text,
    @max_iterations integer default 5
  OUTPUT: @result text
DO
  @iteration := 0
  --  @max_iterations := 5

  -- Initial draft
  GENERATE draft(@task) INTO @current

  -- Iterative refinement loop
  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    GENERATE critique(@current) INTO @feedback

    EVALUATE @feedback
      WHEN 'satisfactory' THEN
        COMMIT @current WITH status = 'complete', iterations = @iteration
      OTHERWISE
        GENERATE refined(@current, @feedback) INTO @current
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
    END
  END

  -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort
  COMMIT @current WITH status = 'max_iterations', iterations = @iteration

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | Status: complete

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: ReAct Agent — Population Growth
-- Fetch a country's population for the past two years via WebSearch,
-- then compute year-over-year growth rate using a Python tool (no LLM math).
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/06_react_agent/react_agent.spl \
--       --adapter claude_cli -m claude-sonnet-4-6 \
--       --allowed-tools WebSearch \
--       --tools cookbook/06_react_agent/tools.py \
--       country="France"

-- Tight prompt for search_population: return only the number, no narration.
CREATE FUNCTION search_population(country TEXT, year INT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Use WebSearch to find the total population of {country} for the year {year}.

Return ONLY the population as a plain integer with no commas, spaces, or text.
Example of valid output: 1409670000
No explanation. No units. No markdown. Just the number.
$$;

-- SPL PROCEDURE: search the web for a country's population in a specific year.
PROCEDURE search_population(country TEXT, year INT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
  GENERATE search_population(country, year) INTO @result
  COMMIT @result
END

-- calc_growth_rate is a Python tool registered from tools.py via --tools.
-- CALL routes to Python directly — deterministic, zero LLM cost.

-- Tight prompt for growth_report: plain narrative, no tool use, no file writes.
CREATE FUNCTION growth_report(country TEXT, year_prev TEXT, pop_prev TEXT, year_curr TEXT, pop_curr TEXT, growth_rate TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Write a concise 2-3 sentence population growth report for {country}.

Data:
- {year_prev} population: {pop_prev}
- {year_curr} population: {pop_curr}
- Year-over-year growth rate: {growth_rate}%

Plain prose only. No markdown headers. No file creation. No tool use. Just the narrative.
$$;

WORKFLOW population_growth
  INPUT: 
    @country TEXT DEFAULT 'China',
    @year_curr integer default 2023
  OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
  @year_prev := @year_curr - 1
  -- @year_curr := 2024

  -- Step 1: Fetch population for each year via WebSearch
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_prev) INTO @pop_prev
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_curr) INTO @pop_curr

  -- Step 2: Compute growth rate — routed to Python tool, not LLM
  CALL calc_growth_rate(@pop_prev, @pop_curr) INTO @growth_rate

  -- Step 3: Compose the final human-readable report
  GENERATE growth_report(@country, @year_prev, @pop_prev, @year_curr, @pop_curr, @growth_rate) INTO @report

  COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
  WHEN SearchFailed THEN
    GENERATE search_error_report(@country) INTO @report
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'error'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 186 in / 119 out
     | Latency: 29936ms

This modest year-over-year growth rate of 0.33% reflects the country's relatively stable demographic trajectory, characteristic of a mature Western European nation. While positive, the pace remains well below replacement-level fertility thresholds, underscoring ongoing challenges around long-term natural population sustainability.
```

Explain how encryption works
```
     | 
```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Safe Generation with Exception Handling
-- Demonstrates LLM-specific error handling patterns

WORKFLOW safe_generation
  INPUT: @prompt text
  OUTPUT: @result text
  SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
  GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result

  -- Quality check via semantic evaluation (LLM-as-judge)
  GENERATE quality_assess(@result) INTO @quality

  EVALUATE @quality
    WHEN 'high_quality' THEN
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'high_quality'
    WHEN 'acceptable' THEN
      GENERATE improved(@result, @prompt) INTO @result
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'refined'
    OTHERWISE
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'best_effort'
  END

EXCEPTION
  WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
    GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'conservative'

  WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
    RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1

  WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
    COMMIT @prompt WITH status = 'refused'

  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'truncated'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 842 in / 719 out
     | Latency: 8937ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Encryption is the process of converting plaintext (readable data) into ciphertext (unreadable data) to ensure confidentiality and security. Here's a step-by-step explanation of how encryption works:

1. **Key Generation**: A key pair is generated, which consists of a public key and a private key. The public key is used for encryption, while the private key is used for decryption.
2. **Encryption Algorithm**: An encryption algorithm, such as AES (Advanced Encryption Standard) or RSA, is applied to the plaintext data using the public key. This transforms the plaintext into ciphertext.
3. **Ciphertext Generation**: The encryption algorithm generates a random number that is added to the plaintext data, which becomes the ciphertext. This ensures that even if an attacker intercepts the ciphertext, they won't be able to extract the original plaintext.
4. **Encryption Protocol**: An encryption protocol, such as SSL/TLS (Secure Sockets Layer/Transport Layer Security), is used to ensure secure communication between parties. This protocol authenticates the identity of both parties and ensures that data is transmitted securely.
5. **Key Exchange**: When a message needs to be decrypted, the private key is used to decrypt the ciphertext using the same encryption algorithm. However, since the public key was shared with the recipient during the initial key exchange process, the recipient can use their own private key to decrypt the message.

Types of Encryption:

* **Symmetric Key Encryption**: Uses the same key for both encryption and decryption.
* **Asymmetric Key Encryption**: Uses a pair of keys (public and private) for encryption and decryption.

Encryption Benefits:

* Protects data confidentiality
* Ensures secure communication over public channels
* Allows for data sharing between parties without revealing sensitive information

Common Applications:

* Secure online transactions (e.g., online banking, e-commerce)
* Confidentiality in email communication
* Data storage and transmission
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @prompt = Explain how encryption works
     |   @quality = **Quality Assessment Report**
     | 
     | **Task:** Quality Assess encryption process
     |

-- Recipe 08: RAG Query
-- Retrieval-augmented generation over indexed documents.
--
-- Setup:
--   pip install numpy faiss-cpu
--   spl rag add /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/zinets/who-is-wen.md
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="Where did Wen grow up?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is Momagrid and why was it built?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the Gang of Four in the ZiNets research team?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is eta_AI and how is it measured?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="How did Wen transition from physics to software engineering?"

PROMPT rag_answer
SELECT
    system_role('You are a knowledgeable assistant. Use the provided context to answer accurately.'),
    rag.query(context.question, top_k=3) AS background,
    context.question AS question
GENERATE answer(question)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 09: Chain of Thought
-- Multi-step reasoning: Research → Analyze → Summarize
-- Each GENERATE feeds into the next via workflow variables.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="distributed AI inference"
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="quantum computing"

WORKFLOW chain_of_thought
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @summary TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research
    GENERATE research(@topic) INTO @research

    -- Step 2: Analyze
    GENERATE analyze(@research) INTO @analysis

    -- Step 3: Summarize
    GENERATE summarize(@analysis) INTO @summary

    COMMIT @summary WITH status = 'complete'
END
```

     | 
     | **What is Distributed AI Inference?**
     | 
     | Distributed AI inference refers to the process of running machine learning (ML) models on multiple computing devices or nodes in parallel, rather than relying on a single powerful computer. This approach enables faster and more efficient processing of complex ML tasks, such as image recognition, natural language processing, and predictive analytics.
     | 
     | **Benefits of Distributed AI Inference**
     | 
     | 1. **Speedup**: By distributing the computation across multiple nodes, distributed AI inference can significantly speed up the training and inference process.
     | 2. **Scalability**: Distributed systems can handle large amounts of data and scale to meet increasing computational demands.
     | 3. **Fault tolerance**: If one node fails, other nodes can continue to operate, reducing downtime and improving overall system reliability.
     | 4. **Cost-effective**: Using multiple computing devices or nodes can reduce the cost of computing resources compared to using a single powerful computer.
     | 
     | **Challenges in Distributed AI Inference**
     | 
     | 1. **Communication overhead**: Communication between nodes can introduce latency and overhead, which can negatively impact performance.
     | 2. **Synchronization**: Ensuring that all nodes are in sync with each other can be challenging, particularly when dealing with complex models.
     | 3. **Data distribution**: Distributing data evenly across nodes can be difficult, especially for large datasets.
     | 
     | **Distributed AI Inference Architectures**
     | 
     | 1. **Master-worker architecture**: A centralized node (master) coordinates the distributed computation, while worker nodes perform the actual computations.
     | 2. **Peer-to-peer architecture**: Nodes are independent and communicate with each other directly to coordinate their work.
     | 3. **Hybrid architecture**: Combines elements of both master-worker and peer-to-peer architectures.
     | 
     | **Technologies Used in Distributed AI Inference**
     | 
     | 1. **Distributed frameworks**: TensorFlow, PyTorch, Hadoop, Spark
     | 2. **Cloud-based services**: AWS SageMaker, Google Cloud AI Platform, Azure Machine Learning
     | 3. **Edge computing**: Accelerates inference on edge devices for real-time applications.

[10] Batch Test
     cmd : spl run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama
     log : /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/SPL20/cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test_20260324_172302.log

```bash
spl run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 10: Batch Test
-- Automated testing of key cookbook recipes across multiple models.
-- Uses parallel CTEs to fan out all recipe × model combinations at once,
-- then generates a structured PASS/FAIL report.
--
-- The bash script (batch_test.sh) is kept for CI/CD and shell-based pipelines.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION summarize_results(
    model_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT,
    r1_name TEXT, r1_m1 TEXT, r1_m2 TEXT,
    r2_name TEXT, r2_m1 TEXT, r2_m2 TEXT,
    r3_name TEXT, r3_m1 TEXT, r3_m2 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a test reporter. Review the outputs below and produce a concise pass/fail report.

A result is PASS if the output is a non-empty, coherent response.
A result is FAIL if the output is empty, contains an error, or is clearly nonsensical.

Models under test: {model_1}, {model_2}

Recipe: {r1_name}
  {model_1}: {r1_m1}
  {model_2}: {r1_m2}

Recipe: {r2_name}
  {model_1}: {r2_m1}
  {model_2}: {r2_m2}

Recipe: {r3_name}
  {model_1}: {r3_m1}
  {model_2}: {r3_m2}

Format each line as:
  PASS  recipe_name  ({model})
  FAIL  recipe_name  ({model})

End with a summary line: "Results: X/Y passed, Z failed"
$$;

WORKFLOW batch_test
    INPUT:
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'llama3.2'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Fan out all recipe × model combinations in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        -- Recipe 01: Hello World
        hello_m1 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        hello_m2 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
        proxy_m1 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        proxy_m2 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 03: Multilingual
        multi_m1 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        multi_m2 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        )

    SELECT
        hello_m1.greeting  AS hello_m1,  hello_m2.greeting  AS hello_m2,
        proxy_m1.answer    AS proxy_m1,  proxy_m2.answer    AS proxy_m2,
        multi_m1.response  AS multi_m1,  multi_m2.response  AS multi_m2
    INTO @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
         @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
         @multi_m1, @multi_m2

    -- Generate the structured pass/fail report
    GENERATE summarize_results(
        @model_1, @model_2,
        '01_hello_world/hello.spl',        @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
        '02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl',        @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
        '03_multilingual/multilingual.spl', @multi_m1, @multi_m2
    ) INTO @report

    COMMIT @report WITH
        status  = 'complete',
        model_1 = @model_1,
        model_2 = @model_2

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'Batch test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 7
     | Tokens: 520 in / 266 out
     | Latency: 3919ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

FAIL      02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl      (gemma3)
PASS      03_multilingual/multilingual.spl (gemma3)
FAIL      02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl      (llama3.2)

Results: 2/2 passed, 0 failed

     | 
     | Do you want me to:
     | 
     | *   Greet a specific person? (e.g., "Greet John!")
     | *   Give a general greeting? (e.g., "Hello!", "Good morning!")
     |   @hello_m2 = Hello! How can I assist you today?

```spl
-- Recipe 11: Debate Arena
-- Two LLM personas argue opposing sides of a topic, then a judge picks the winner.
-- Demonstrates multi-perspective reasoning, adversarial generation, and semantic evaluation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="AI should be open-sourced"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="Remote work is better than office work"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama topic="Tabs are better than spaces"

CREATE FUNCTION pro_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY IN FAVOR of the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument supporting the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION con_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY AGAINST the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument opposing the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION judge_debate(topic TEXT, pro_history TEXT, con_history TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are an impartial debate judge evaluating the following debate.

Motion: "{topic}"

--- PRO SIDE (arguing IN FAVOR) ---
{pro_history}

--- CON SIDE (arguing AGAINST) ---
{con_history}

Evaluate the debate on these criteria:
1. Strength of arguments
2. Quality of rebuttals
3. Clarity and persuasiveness

Declare a winner (PRO or CON) and explain your reasoning in 2-3 paragraphs.
$$;

WORKFLOW debate_arena
    INPUT: 
        @topic TEXT,
        @max_rounds integer DEFAULT 3
    OUTPUT: @verdict TEXT
DO
    @round := 0
    @pro_history := ''
    @con_history := ''

    -- Opening statements
    GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @pro
    GENERATE con_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @con

    @pro_history := @pro
    @con_history := @con

    -- Rebuttal rounds
    WHILE @round < @max_rounds DO
        -- Pro rebuts Con's latest point
        GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, @con_history) INTO @pro_rebuttal
        @pro_history := @pro_history + '\n---\n' + @pro_rebuttal

        -- Con rebuts Pro's latest point
        GENERATE con_argument(@topic, @pro_history) INTO @con_rebuttal
        @con_history := @con_history + '\n---\n' + @con_rebuttal

        @round := @round + 1
    END

    -- Judge evaluates both sides
    GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict

    COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'complete', rounds = @round

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict
        COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @pro_history WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 9
     | Tokens: 8863 in / 2922 out
     | Latency: 43889ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

The PRO side also provided compelling examples of successful open-source projects, such as Linux, which have become cornerstones of modern computing. Their arguments were clear, concise, and persuasive, making a strong case for the benefits of open-sourcing AI.

While they raised legitimate concerns about potential risks, their rebuttals often relied on flawed assumptions or overlooked the complexities of AI systems. The CON side's arguments also seemed to be driven by a desire to maintain the status quo of proprietary development, rather than addressing the benefits of open-sourcing.

Overall, while both sides presented valid points, the PRO side demonstrated a stronger understanding of the benefits and risks associated with open-sourcing AI technology. Their arguments were more nuanced, comprehensive, and persuasive, making them the winner in this debate.
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @topic = AI should be open-sourced

     | 
     | The current state of AI development is marked by secrecy and exclusivity. Tech giants like Google, Microsoft, and Facebook hoard their AI advancements, locking them behind paywalls and proprietary protocols. This approach stifles innovation, as researchers are discouraged from sharing knowledge or collaborating with others. The result is a fragmented landscape where AI capabilities are duplicated across companies, rather than being leveraged for the greater good.
     |

This would accelerate progress in areas like healthcare, education, and environmental sustainability.      |

With proper governance and licensing agreements, open-source AI can be designed to prioritize both innovation and safety. The benefits of open-sourcing far outweigh the perceived risks. We owe it to ourselves, our children, and future generations to harness the full potential of AI for the betterment of humanity.

     | 
     | Firstly, the claim that open-sourcing AI algorithms and models would grant sensitive information to unvetted individuals and organizations is unfounded. Open-sourced AI projects are typically accompanied by strict licensing agreements that ensure the responsible use and sharing of intellectual property. Moreover, the community-driven nature of open-source AI development fosters a culture of transparency and accountability, which would be hard to replicate in proprietary systems. By contrast, proprietary systems often have opaque governance structures and limited access to information, making it more difficult to identify and mitigate potential security vulnerabilities.
     | 
     | Furthermore, my opponent's assertion that open-sourcing AI technology would lead to a lack of accountability and oversight is misguided. These same safeguards can be replicated in proprietary systems, but they would require significant changes to the existing business models and culture.
     | 
     | Finally, my opponent's criticism of open-sourcing as a threat to responsible innovation is symptomatic of a broader failure to recognize the value of collaboration and knowledge-sharing in driving progress. By opening up AI technology to the public domain, we can accelerate the pace of innovation, foster new ideas and applications, and create a more inclusive and equitable technological landscape. The benefits of open-sourcing far outweigh the hypothetical risks, and it is our responsibility as leaders and citizens to prioritize the common good over the interests of entrenched industries.
     | ---
     | My opponent presents a laundry list of concerns about open-sourcing AI technology, but their arguments are fundamentally flawed and misguided.
     | 
     | Firstly, the notion that open-sourcing AI algorithms and models would grant sensitive information to unvetted individuals and organizations is an exaggeration. While it's true that some malicious actors might exploit vulnerabilities, this can be mitigated through effective vetting processes, encryption, and secure communication protocols.      |

These mechanisms have already proven effective in ensuring the quality and integrity of open-source software.
     | 
     | The claim that open-sourcing AI technology would undermine progress in developing and deploying this technology responsibly is also unfounded. In fact, open-source approaches like Linux have become a cornerstone of modern computing, demonstrating that collaborative development can lead to greater innovation and efficiency. By embracing open-source principles, we can create a more vibrant ecosystem of developers working together to advance AI research and application.
     | 
     | Ultimately, the benefits of open-sourcing AI technology far outweigh the risks. By promoting transparency, collaboration, and community involvement, we can unlock new possibilities for AI development that would be impossible through proprietary development alone. It's time to break free from the siloed approach of traditional proprietary development and harness the collective genius of the global developer community to create a brighter future for AI research and application.

By contrast, open-sourcing AI algorithms and models would grant the global community access to valuable knowledge and expertise, fostering collaboration, innovation, and progress.

     | The notion that self-regulation efforts within the open-source community can address concerns about security and accountability is naive at best. Instead of relying on the benevolence of the open-source community, we should prioritize robust governance structures and industry-led initiatives to ensure accountability and oversight in AI development. The establishment of clear standards, rigorous testing protocols, and a culture of transparency and disclosure would provide the necessary safeguards to mitigate risks.
     | 
     | Furthermore, open-sourcing AI technology would not compromise intellectual property protection or national security. This suggests that responsible development practices can mitigate any potential vulnerabilities. We must seize this opportunity and prioritize the greater good over outdated concerns about secrecy and security.

The burden of responsibility for these unintended consequences would fall on individual developers, who may not have the necessary expertise or resources to mitigate them.

My opponent's claims of a "culture of transparency" in the open-source community ring hollow when considering the complexities of software development, particularly in high-stakes domains such as defense.
     |

Anything less would be a betrayal of our responsibility as stewards of this powerful technology.

This suggests that the risks associated with open-sourcing AI are overstated, and that responsible development practices can mitigate any potential vulnerabilities.
     | 
     | Furthermore, my opponent's argument that open-sourcing would lead to a lack of accountability is based on a false dichotomy between openness and control. In reality, open-source projects often feature rigorous testing, peer review, and auditing processes, which are no less effective than those employed in proprietary systems. Moreover, the accountability mechanism provided by licensing agreements and community scrutiny can be just as effective in ensuring responsible development practices.
     | 
     | Ultimately, my opponent's argument relies on a utopian vision of an AI development landscape where collaboration and transparency somehow magically outweigh concerns about secrecy, security, and intellectual property protection. Moreover, the assumption that a vetting process can detect all potential security vulnerabilities overlooks the complexity of AI systems.
     |

But let us not be fooled by the false promises of proprietary development or rely on outdated notions about secrecy and security. We must prioritize the greater good over the interests of entrenched industries.

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 12: Plan and Execute
-- A planner agent decomposes a task into steps, then an executor implements each one.
-- When output_dir is set, generated code files are written to disk one-by-one.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       --tools cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/tools.py \
--       task="Build a REST API for a todo app" \
--       output_dir="cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/output"
--
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       task="Set up a CI/CD pipeline for a Python project"


-- Phase 1: Decompose task into a numbered implementation plan
CREATE FUNCTION plan(task TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software architect. Break down this task into clear implementation steps:

Task: {task}

Output a numbered list of concrete steps (3–6 steps). Each step should be specific
and independently executable. No prose — just the numbered list.
$$;

-- Count how many steps are in the plan (capped at max_steps)
CREATE FUNCTION count_steps(plan TEXT, max_steps TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Count the number of numbered steps in this plan.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.
If there are more than {max_steps} steps, return {max_steps}.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Extract step N from the plan (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_step(plan TEXT, step_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Extract step number {step_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered plan and return
ONLY that step's description. No numbering prefix. No extra text.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Execute a step: produce a concise design decision + list of files to create
CREATE FUNCTION execute_step(current_step TEXT, prior_results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer planning an implementation.

Step: {current_step}
Completed so far: {prior_results}

Describe in 2-3 sentences what this step produces and list the filenames it creates.
Be brief — no code yet, just the design decision and file list.
$$;

-- Validate that a step produced meaningful output
CREATE FUNCTION validate_step(current_step TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Did the following implementation successfully address the step?

Step: {current_step}
Implementation: {step_result}

Reply with exactly one word: "passed" or "failed".
$$;

-- Re-plan after a step failure
CREATE FUNCTION replan(task TEXT, plan TEXT, step_index TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
A step in our implementation plan failed. Create a revised plan.

Original task: {task}
Original plan: {plan}
Failed at step: {step_index}
Failure details: {step_result}

Output a new numbered plan starting from step {step_index}. Keep completed steps as-is.
$$;

-- Phase 4a: List all source files to create (no code — just filenames + one-line descriptions)
CREATE FUNCTION outline_files(task TEXT, results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Based on the implementation plan below, list every
source file that must be created to complete the task.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

Output a numbered list — one file per line — with the filename and a one-sentence description.
Always include a README.md as the last item.
Example:
1. app.py - Flask application entry point, registers blueprints and starts the server
2. models.py - SQLAlchemy ORM models for Todo and User
3. README.md - Setup instructions, project layout, and example API calls

No code. No prose. Just the numbered file list.
$$;

-- Count files in the outline
CREATE FUNCTION count_files(outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Count the number of numbered items in this file list.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Extract one file entry at index N (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_file(outline TEXT, file_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Extract item number {file_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered file list and return
ONLY that item's filename and description. No number prefix. No extra text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Generate complete code for ONE file
CREATE FUNCTION generate_file(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_desc TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Write complete, runnable code for ONE source file.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

File to create: {file_desc}

Output a SINGLE fenced code block. The FIRST line inside the fence MUST be a filename comment:

```python
# filename: app/main.py
...complete code...
```
     | 
     | Requirements:
     | - Complete, runnable code — no placeholders, no TODOs.
     | - Error handling, type hints, brief inline comments.
     | - Only this one file — nothing else.
     | $$;
     | 
     | -- Summarize what was built
     | CREATE FUNCTION summarize(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
     | In 3-5 sentences, describe what was built for this task and how to run it.
     | 
     | Task: {task}
     | Files created:
     | {file_outline}
     | $$;
     | 
     | 
     | WORKFLOW plan_and_execute
     |     INPUT:
     |         @task TEXT DEFAULT 'Build a REST API for a todo app',
     |         @output_dir TEXT DEFAULT '',
     |         @max_steps INTEGER DEFAULT 5
     |     OUTPUT: @final_report TEXT
     | DO
     |     @step_index := 0
     |     @results := ''
     | 
     |     -- Phase 1: Plan
     |     GENERATE plan(@task) INTO @plan
     | 
     |     -- Phase 2: Count steps (capped at max_steps)
     |     GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     | 
     |     -- Phase 3: Execute each step (brief design notes only — no code)
     |     WHILE @step_index < @step_count DO
     |         GENERATE extract_step(@plan, @step_index) INTO @current_step
     |         GENERATE execute_step(@current_step, @results) INTO @step_result
     |         GENERATE validate_step(@current_step, @step_result) INTO @validation
     | 
     |         EVALUATE @validation
     |             WHEN 'failed' THEN
     |                 GENERATE replan(@task, @plan, @step_index, @step_result) INTO @plan
     |                 GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     |                 @step_index := 0
     |                 @results := ''
     |             OTHERWISE
     |                 @results := @results + '\n## Step ' + @step_index + '\n' + @step_result
     |                 @step_index := @step_index + 1
     |         END
     |     END
     | 
     |     -- Phase 4: Outline all files to generate
     |     GENERATE outline_files(@task, @results) INTO @file_outline
     |     GENERATE count_files(@file_outline) INTO @file_count
     | 
     |     -- Phase 5: Generate each file individually (one LLM call per file — avoids truncation)
     |     @file_index := 0
     |     WHILE @file_index < @file_count DO
     |         GENERATE extract_file(@file_outline, @file_index) INTO @current_file
     |         GENERATE generate_file(@task, @results, @current_file) INTO @file_code
     |         CALL write_code_files(@file_code, @output_dir) INTO @file_written
     |         @file_index := @file_index + 1
     |     END
     | 
     |     -- Phase 6: Summary report
     |     GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     | 
     |     COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'complete', steps_executed = @step_index, files_generated = @file_count
     | 
     | EXCEPTION
     |     WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
     |         GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     |         COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'partial', steps_executed = @step_index
     |     WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
     |         COMMIT @results WITH status = 'budget_limit'
     | END
     | ```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 243
     | Tokens: 87926 in / 35323 out
     | Latency: 508288ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
A REST API was built for a todo app using Flask, a lightweight Python web framework. The API consists of endpoints for creating new todo items, as well as controllers for handling these requests. To run the application, navigate to the project root directory and execute `python app.py` to start the server. Then, use a tool like curl or Postman to make API calls to interact with the todo items, such as `curl -X POST http://localhost:5000/todos` to create a new todo item.
```

     | ## Step 4
     | This step defines the RESTful API endpoints for managing todo items, including POST /todos to create a new item, GET /todos to retrieve all items, PUT /todos/:id to update an existing item, and DELETE /todos/:id to delete an item.
     | 
     | Filenames created:
     | 
     | - Project Scope Document (PDF)
     | - Project Plan Outline (Word document)
     | - Risk Assessment Report (Excel spreadsheet)
     |   @plan = **Revised Plan for Building a REST API for a Todo App**
     | 
     | 1. **Review and Re-evaluate Project Scope and Objectives**
     |    - Conduct a thorough review of the project's scope and objectives.
     |    - Identify any changes or adjustments needed to ensure alignment with the revised plan.
     | 
     | 2. Set up an alternative database migration tool:
     |    - Choose an alternative database migration tool (e.g., Alembic or Octopedal) and set it up according to the documentation.
     |    - Verify that the tool is properly configured and working correctly.
     | 
     | 3. Install Node.js and required dependencies:
     |    - Run npm install express body-parser jsonwebtoken bcryptjs dotenv to resolve issues.
     |    - Verify that all dependencies are installed correctly.
     | 
     | 4. Implement API endpoints for CRUD operations on todo items:
     |    - Define POST /todos endpoint to create a new todo item
     |    - Define GET /todos endpoint to retrieve all todo items
     |    - Define PUT /todos/:id endpoint to update a todo item
     |    - Define DELETE /todos/:id endpoint to delete a todo item
     | 
     | 5. Implement API endpoints for CRUD operations on users:
     |    - Define POST /users endpoint to create a new user
     |    - Define GET /users endpoint to retrieve all users
     |    - Define PUT /users/:id endpoint to update a user
     |    - Define DELETE /users/:id endpoint to delete a user
     | 
     | 6. Test and deploy the API using a framework like Postman or cURL:
     |     - Write test cases for each endpoint using a testing library (e.g., Jest, Mocha).
     |     - Validate output data and error responses.
     |     - Deploy to a cloud platform (e.g., Heroku, AWS) with an alternative setup.
     | 
     | 7. Implement server.js file with Express setup:
     |    - Import required dependencies in server.js (express, bodyParser, jwt, bcrypt, dotenv).
     |    - Initialize Express app and set up router for todo API endpoints.
     | 
     | 8. Define routes and handlers for API endpoints:
     |     - Use the setup from step 6 to define routes and handlers for each endpoint
     | 
     | 9. Implement database interactions for API endpoints:
     |    - Use the chosen migration tool to apply migrations and create the schema.
     |    - Create functions to create, read, update, and delete (CRUD) todo items and users from the database
     | 
     | 10. Test and validate API endpoints:
     |     - Use Postman or cURL to test each endpoint
     |     - Validate output data and error responses using a testing library (e.g., Jest, Mocha)
     | 
     | 11. Implement authentication and authorization for API endpoints:
     |    - Use JWT tokens to verify user authenticity and authorize access to protected routes
     | 
     | 12. Test authentication and authorization flow:
     |     - Test successful login and registration
     |     - Test invalid login attempts
     |     - Test unauthorized access to protected routes
     | 
     | 13. Refactor code to improve maintainability and scalability:
     |     - Extract functions to create, read, update, and delete todo items and users into separate modules.
     |     - Use dependency injection to manage dependencies between modules.
     |     - Implement logging and monitoring for the API
     | 
     | 14. Review and refine implementation based on findings from testing and validation:
     |    - Refine API endpoints and database schema as needed
     |    - Address any issues or discrepancies found during testing and validation
     | 
     | 15. Finalize and deliver the REST API for the Todo App:
     |    - Ensure all features are implemented and tested
     |    - Verify that the API is secure, scalable, and maintainable
     |    - Document API usage and implementation details for future reference
     | 
     | **Changes Made**

```python
# filename: app/main.py

from flask import Flask, request, jsonify
import uuid

app = Flask(__name__)

class TodoApp:
    def __init__(self):
        self._todos = []

    @property
    def todos(self):
        return self._todos.copy()

    def create_todo(self, data):
        """
        Create a new todo item.

        Request body:
            - title: str
            - description: str

        Returns:
            - todo_id: uuid.UUID
            - message: str
        """
        if not data or 'title' not in data or 'description' not in data:
            return jsonify({'message': 'Missing required fields'}), 400
        
        new_todo = {
            'id': str(uuid.uuid4()),
            'title': data['title'],
            'description': data['description']
        }
        
        self._todos.append(new_todo)
        return jsonify(new_todo), 201

    def get_all_todos(self):
        """
        Retrieve all todo items.

        Returns:
            - list: dict
                - id (uuid.UUID): Todo item ID
                - title (str): Todo item title
                - description (str): Todo item description
        """
        return jsonify(self.todos)

    def update_todo(self, todo_id, data):
        """
        Update an existing todo item.

        Path parameter:
            - todo_id: uuid.UUID

        Request body:
            - title: str
            - description: str

        Returns:
            - message: str
        """
        for todo in self._todos:
            if todo['id'] == todo_id:
                if 'title' in data and 'description' in data:
                    todo['title'] = data['title']
                    todo['description'] = data['description']
                return jsonify({'message': 'Todo item updated successfully'})
        
        return jsonify({'message': 'Todo item not found'}), 404

    def delete_todo(self, todo_id):
        """
        Delete a todo item.

        Path parameter:
            - todo_id: uuid.UUID

        Returns:
            - message: str
        """
        for todo in self._todos:
            if todo['id'] == todo_id:
                self._todos.remove(todo)
                return jsonify({'message': 'Todo item deleted successfully'})
        
        return jsonify({'message': 'Todo item not found'}), 404

def create_app():
    app = Flask(__name__)
    app.config["DEBUG"] = True
    
    todos_app = TodoApp()
    
    @app.route('/todos', methods=['POST'])
    def create_todo_route():
        data = request.get_json()
        return todos_app.create_todo(data)

    @app.route('/todos', methods=['GET'])
    def get_all_todos_route():
        return todos_app.get_all_todos()

    @app.route('/todos/<todo_id>', methods=['PUT'])
    def update_todo_route(todo_id):
        data = request.get_json()
        return todos_app.update_todo(todo_id, data)

    @app.route('/todos/<todo_id>', methods=['DELETE'])
    def delete_todo_route(todo_id):
        return todos_app.delete_todo(todo_id)
    
    app.register_blueprint({
        'root': {
            'url_prefix': '/',
            'route': '/todos'
        }
    })
    
    return app

if __name__ == '__main__':
    app = create_app()
    app.run(debug=True)
```
     | 
     | Changes made:
     | 
     | 1.  Class `TodoApp` was created to encapsulate the todo items and their operations.
     | 2.  The todos are stored in a list (`self._todos`) inside the class, which is accessed via the property `todos`.
     | 3.  Improved documentation using docstrings for each method.
     | 4.  Method names were changed from verb-noun style to noun-verb style for better readability.
     | 5.  Added `create_app` function that initializes a new Flask application instance and sets up the routes.
     | 6.  Used a dictionary in `app.register_blueprint()` to customize the route prefix, which makes it easier to manage different blueprints in the application.
     | 
     | These changes improve code organization, maintainability, and readability while providing better structure for managing todo items using a more object-oriented approach.

```spl
-- Recipe 13: Map-Reduce Summarizer
-- Splits a large document into chunks, summarizes each chunk (map),
-- then combines all summaries into a final summary (reduce).
-- Demonstrates the map-reduce pattern for handling content beyond context limits.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 document="$(cat large_doc.txt)"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama document="$(cat README.md)" style="bullet points"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama document="long text here..." style="executive brief"

WORKFLOW map_reduce_summarizer
    INPUT: @document TEXT, @style TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_summary TEXT
DO
    @chunk_index := 0
    @summaries := ''

    -- Step 1: Determine how to split the document
    GENERATE chunk_plan(@document) INTO @chunk_count

    -- Step 2: MAP — summarize each chunk independently
    WHILE @chunk_index < @chunk_count DO
        GENERATE extract_chunk(@document, @chunk_index, @chunk_count) INTO @chunk

        GENERATE summarize_chunk(@chunk, @chunk_index) INTO @chunk_summary

        @summaries := @summaries + '\n[Chunk ' + @chunk_index + ']: ' + @chunk_summary
        @chunk_index := @chunk_index + 1
    END

    -- Step 3: REDUCE — combine all chunk summaries into one
    GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary

    -- Step 4: Quality check the final summary
    GENERATE quality_score(@final_summary, @document) INTO @score

    EVALUATE @score
        WHEN > 0.7 THEN
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'complete', chunks = @chunk_count
        OTHERWISE
            GENERATE improve_summary(@final_summary, @summaries) INTO @final_summary
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'refined', chunks = @chunk_count
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- If document is too large even for chunking, do hierarchical reduction
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

Here is an improved version of the `reduce_summaries` function:

```python
def reduce_summaries(input_text, summary_points):
    """
    Reduces summaries into bullet points.

    Args:
        input_text (str): The text to be reduced.
        summary_points (dict): A dictionary with summary keys and corresponding values.

    Returns:
        str: A string containing the reduced summaries in bullet point format.
    """

    # Initialize an empty list to store the formatted bullet points
    bullet_points_list = []

    # Split the input_text into sentences or phrases
    for i, sentence in enumerate(input_text.split('. ')):
        # Add each bullet point to the list with its corresponding key from summary_points
        bullet_points_list.append(f"{i+1}. {summary_points[sentence]}")

    # Return a string containing all bullet points separated by commas and newline characters
    return ',\n'.join(bullet_points_list)
```
     | 
     | This improved version includes the following changes:
     | 
     | - Replaced `input` with `input_text` for clarity.
     | - Renamed `bullet_points` to `summary_points` for better accuracy in describing its purpose.
     | - Used f-strings to format each bullet point with its corresponding key from `summary_points`.
     | - Added a newline character after each bullet point and before the next one, making it more visually appealing.
     | 
     | Here's an example usage of the improved function:
     | 
```python
input_text = "The company will release new products. The company will also expand its services."
summary_points = {
    'Release New Products': input_text.split('. ')[0].strip(),
    'Expand Services': input_text.split('. ')[1].strip()
}

print(reduce_summaries(input_text, summary_points))
```
     | 
     | This output will be:
     | 
     | ```
     | 1. Release New Products
     | 2. Expand Services
     | ```
     | ```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @document = The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog. This is a test document for map-reduce summarization.
     |   @style = bullet points
     |   @chunk_index = 0
     |   @chunk_count = import re
     | from nltk.tokenize import word_tokenize, sent_tokenize
     | from nltk.corpus import stopwords
     | from collections import Counter
     | 
     | def chunk_plan(input_string):
     |     # Step 1: Tokenize the input string into words and sentences
     |     words = word_tokenize(input_string)
     |     sentences = sent_tokenize(input_string)
     | 
     |     # Step 2: Preprocess the words by converting them to lowercase, removing punctuation, and removing stopwords
     |     stop_words = set(stopwords.words('english'))
     |     words = [word.lower() for word in words if word.isalpha() and word not in stop_words]
     | 
     |     # Step 3: Count the frequency of each word
     |     word_freq = Counter(words)
     | 
     |     # Step 4: Identify the top N most frequent words
     |     n = 10  # adjust this value to get a summary of different lengths
     |     top_n_words = word_freq.most_common(n)
     | 
     |     # Step 5: Chunk the input string based on the top N most frequent words
     |     chunked_string = ""
     |     current_chunk = []
     |     for sentence in sentences:
     |         for word in word_tokenize(sentence):
     |             if word.lower() in [w[0] for w in top_n_words]:
     |                 if current_chunk:
     |                     chunked_string += ' '.join(current_chunk) + '\n'
     |                     current_chunk = []
     |                 chunked_string += word + ' '
     |                 current_chunk.append(word)
     |         if current_chunk:
     |             chunked_string += ' '.join(current_chunk) + '\n'
     |             current_chunk = []
     | 
     |     # Step 6: Add the last chunk to the summary
     |     if current_chunk:
     |         chunked_string += ' '.join(current_chunk)
     | 
     |     return chunked_string
     |   @score = It seems like your `reduce_summaries` function does not accurately capture the main points of the input text, but rather splits it into individual sentences or phrases. 
     | 
     | To improve this function, we can use Natural Language Processing (NLP) techniques to identify the main ideas in the input text and then extract the key information from those ideas.
     | 
     | Here is an example of how you could modify your `reduce_summaries` function using NLTK library's sent_tokenize to split the text into sentences:
     | 
```python
import nltk

nltk.download('punkt')

def reduce_summaries(input, bullet_points):
    """
    Reduces summaries into bullet points.

    Args:
        input (str): The text to be reduced.
        bullet_points (dict): A dictionary with summary keys and corresponding values.

    Returns:
        str: A string containing the reduced summaries in bullet point format.
    """

    # Initialize an empty list to store the main ideas
    main_ideas = []

    # Split the input into sentences
    for sentence in nltk.sent_tokenize(input):
        # Check if the sentence is not a period or a comma
        if sentence.strip() != "." and sentence.strip() != ",":
            # Add the sentence to the list of main ideas
            main_ideas.append(sentence.strip())

    # Initialize an empty list to store the bullet points
    bullet_points_list = []

    # Iterate over each key-value pair in the bullet_points dictionary
    for key, value in bullet_points.items():
        # Check if the value is present in the input text
        if value.lower() in main_ideas:
            # Add the sentence as a bullet point to the list
            bullet_points_list.append(f"- {key}: {value}")

    # Return a string containing all bullet points separated by commas
    return ', '.join(bullet_points_list)


# Example usage:
input_text = "The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog. This is a test document for map-reduce summarization."

print(reduce_summaries(input_text, {
    'Key Idea 1': input_text.split('. ')[0].strip(),
    'Key Idea 2': input_text.split('. ')[1].strip()
}))
```
     | 
     | This modified function uses NLTK's `sent_tokenize` to split the text into sentences. It then checks each sentence in the input text against the key-value pairs in the `bullet_points` dictionary and adds any matching sentences as bullet points.

```spl
-- Recipe 14: Multi-Agent Collaboration
-- Specialized agents (Researcher, Analyst, Writer) collaborate on a report.
-- Each agent has a distinct role and passes results to the next.
-- Demonstrates procedural decomposition, CALL-based delegation, and role specialization.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="Impact of AI on healthcare"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="Future of renewable energy"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="State of quantum computing in 2025"

-- Agent 1: Researcher — gathers key facts and sources
PROCEDURE researcher(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE research_facts(topic) INTO @facts
    GENERATE identify_key_themes(@facts) INTO @themes
    @result := @facts + '\n\nKey Themes:\n' + @themes
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 2: Analyst — identifies trends, risks, and opportunities
PROCEDURE analyst(research TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE analyze_trends(research) INTO @trends
    GENERATE assess_risks(research, topic) INTO @risks
    GENERATE find_opportunities(research, topic) INTO @opportunities
    @result := 'Trends:\n' + @trends + '\n\nRisks:\n' + @risks + '\n\nOpportunities:\n' + @opportunities
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 3: Writer — produces polished final report
PROCEDURE writer(research TEXT, analysis TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE draft_report(topic, research, analysis) INTO @draft
    GENERATE critique(@draft) INTO @feedback
    GENERATE revise_report(@draft, @feedback) INTO @final
    COMMIT @final
END

-- Orchestrator workflow
WORKFLOW multi_agent_report
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Delegate to specialized agents
    CALL researcher(@topic) INTO @research
    CALL analyst(@research, @topic) INTO @analysis
    CALL writer(@research, @analysis, @topic) INTO @report

    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @research WITH status = 'partial_research_only'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 8

Here is a revised version of the draft report:

**Title:** The Impact of AI on Healthcare: Opportunities, Challenges, and Emerging Trends

**Executive Summary:**
Artificial intelligence (AI) is transforming the healthcare industry by improving patient outcomes, streamlining clinical workflows, and enhancing decision-making capabilities. However, AI also poses significant challenges and risks, including data quality and bias, regulatory frameworks, cybersecurity threats, workforce displacement, and explainability and transparency. This report provides a comprehensive overview of the current state of AI in healthcare, its potential benefits and risks, as well as emerging trends and opportunities for growth.

**Introduction:**
The use of Artificial Intelligence (AI) in healthcare is no longer a novelty but an established reality. The applications of AI are diverse and varied, including early disease detection, personalized medicine, and enhanced patient safety. However, the widespread adoption of AI in healthcare also raises important questions about its impact on the industry, particularly in terms of data quality, regulatory frameworks, cybersecurity threats, workforce displacement, and explainability and transparency.

**Positive Impacts:**

1. **Early Disease Detection:** AI-powered algorithms can analyze large amounts of medical data to detect diseases such as cancer at an early stage, when treatment is more effective.
2. **Personalized Medicine:** AI can help personalize treatment plans by analyzing individual patient data and identifying the most effective treatments based on their unique characteristics.
3. **Streamlined Clinical Workflows:** AI-powered chatbots and virtual assistants can help reduce administrative burdens and free up healthcare professionals to focus on high-value tasks.
4. **Improved Diagnosis Accuracy:** AI-assisted image analysis and pattern recognition algorithms can improve diagnostic accuracy for conditions like diabetic retinopathy and cardiovascular disease.
5. **Enhanced Patient Safety:** AI-powered predictive analytics can identify patients at high risk of hospital readmission or complications, enabling proactive interventions to prevent these outcomes.

**Challenges and Limitations:**

1. **Data Quality and Bias:** AI algorithms are only as good as the data they're trained on. Poor data quality and biased training datasets can lead to inaccurate or unfair results.
2. **Regulatory Frameworks:** The lack of clear regulatory guidelines for AI in healthcare creates uncertainty around liability, accountability, and data protection.
3. **Cybersecurity Risks:** As AI becomes more pervasive in healthcare, cybersecurity threats become a growing concern, potentially compromising patient confidentiality and safety.
4. **Workforce Displacement:** Automation and AI may displace some medical jobs, particularly those involving routine or repetitive tasks.
5. **Explainability and Transparency:** As AI assumes more decision-making roles in healthcare, there is a need for greater explainability and transparency around AI-driven decisions.

**Emerging Trends:**

1. **Natural Language Processing (NLP):** NLP can help analyze unstructured medical data from patient notes, social media, and other sources.
2. **Robotics and Telemedicine:** Robots and telemedicine platforms are being used to enhance remote monitoring and patient engagement.
3. **Synthetic Data Generation:** Synthetic data generation techniques can help address data scarcity and variability in healthcare applications.
4. **Value-Based Care:** AI is increasingly being used to optimize value-based care by analyzing costs, outcomes, and other factors to identify areas for improvement.

**Opportunities:**

1. **Developing NLP Capabilities:** Investing in NLP can help analyze unstructured medical data from patient notes, social media, and other sources.
2. **Robotics and Telemedicine Integration:** Collaborating with robotics and telemedicine platforms can enhance remote monitoring and patient engagement.
3. **Addressing Data Scarcity and Variability:** Synthetic data generation techniques can help address data scarcity and variability in healthcare applications.

**Risks:**

1. **Data Quality and Bias:** Poor data quality and biased training datasets can lead to inaccurate or unfair results.
2. **Regulatory Frameworks:** The lack of clear regulatory guidelines for AI in healthcare creates uncertainty around liability, accountability, and data protection.
3. **Cybersecurity Risks:** As AI becomes more pervasive in healthcare, cybersecurity threats become a growing concern, potentially compromising patient confidentiality and safety.
4. **Workforce Displacement:** Automation and AI may displace some medical jobs, particularly those involving routine or repetitive tasks.
5. **Explainability and Transparency:** As AI assumes more decision-making roles in healthcare, there is a need for greater explainability and transparency around AI-driven decisions.

**Emerging Risks:**

1. **NLP and Social Media Analysis:** The increasing use of NLP to analyze unstructured medical data from patient notes, social media, and other sources may introduce new risks, such as misinformation or biased analysis.
2. **Synthetic Data Generation and Data Scarcity:** Synthetic data generation techniques can help address data scarcity, but there is a risk that these methods may not deliver accurate results.

**Strategies for Mitigating Risks:**

1.

```spl
-- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
-- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
-- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="$(cat myfile.py)"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)" language="Go"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)" language="Python"

WORKFLOW code_review
    INPUT: @code TEXT, @language TEXT
    OUTPUT: @review TEXT
DO
    -- Pass 1: Security audit
    GENERATE security_audit(@code, @language) INTO @security_findings

    -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
    GENERATE performance_review(@code, @language) INTO @perf_findings

    -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
    GENERATE style_review(@code, @language) INTO @style_findings

    -- Pass 4: Bug detection
    GENERATE bug_detection(@code, @language) INTO @bug_findings

    -- Severity scoring for each category
    GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score

    -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
    GENERATE synthesize_review(
        @security_findings, @sec_score,
        @perf_findings, @perf_score,
        @style_findings,
        @bug_findings, @bug_score
    ) INTO @review

    -- Determine overall verdict
    EVALUATE @sec_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
        WHEN > 5 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
        OTHERWISE
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Code too large — review in chunks
        GENERATE summarize_code(@code) INTO @summary
        GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
        COMMIT @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
END
```

     |   @security_findings = **Security Audit Report**
     | 
     | The provided code contains a critical vulnerability that makes it susceptible to severe security risks.
     | 
     | ### Input Validation and Sanitization
     | 
     | The function `foo` takes user input `x` and passes it directly to the `eval()` function. This is a significant concern because `eval()` allows the execution of arbitrary code, which can lead to:
     | 
     | 1.  **Code Injection**: An attacker could inject malicious code, potentially leading to data corruption, unauthorized access, or even system compromise.
     | 2.  **Denial of Service (DoS)**: Malicious input could cause an infinite loop or consume excessive resources, resulting in a denial of service.
     | 
     | **Recommendation**
     | 
     | To address this vulnerability:
     | 
     | 1.  **Input Validation**: Only accept and process trusted user input to prevent malicious code injection.
     | 2.  **Code Sanitization**: Use a safe evaluation method that filters out potentially malicious characters.
     | 
     | ### Improved Code
     | 
```python
import asteval

def foo(x):
    """
    Evaluates a mathematical expression with sanitised input.

    Args:
        x (str): The user-provided input (a string containing a mathematical expression).

    Returns:
        float: The result of the evaluated expression.
    """
    # Create an instance of the asteval Interpreter
    aeval = asteval.Interpreter()

    try:
        # Evaluate the expression and return the result
        return aeval(x)
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Failed to evaluate expression: {e}")
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")
```
     | 
     | ### Alternative Solution (using `numexpr`)
     | 
     | Alternatively, you can use the `numexpr` library, which provides a safe way to evaluate mathematical expressions.
     | 
```python
import numexpr as ne

def foo(x):
    """
    Evaluates a mathematical expression with sanitised input using numexpr.

    Args:
        x (str): The user-provided input (a string containing a mathematical expression).

    Returns:
        float: The result of the evaluated expression.
    """
    try:
        # Evaluate the expression and return the result
        return ne.evaluate(x)
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Failed to evaluate expression: {e}")
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")
```
     | 
     | **Best Practices**
     | 
     | 1.  Always validate user input before processing it.
     | 2.  Use safe evaluation methods, such as `asteval` or `numexpr`, instead of `eval()`.
     | 3.  Limit the scope and privilege of the evaluated code to prevent potential security risks.
     | 
     | By following these guidelines and using safe evaluation methods, you can significantly reduce the risk of security vulnerabilities in your code.

```python
import ast

def evaluate_expression(x: str) -> any:
    """
    Evaluate a string expression using AST.

    Args:
        x (str): The expression to be evaluated.

    Returns:
        any: The result of the evaluation.
    """
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except ValueError as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid input: {e}")
```
     | 
     | **Example Usage:**
     | 
```python

result = evaluate_expression(expression)
print(result)  # Output: 3

try:
    invalid_expression = " invalid expression "
    result = evaluate_expression(invalid_expression)
except ValueError as e:
    print(e)  # Output: Invalid input: expected string, but found '<class 'str'>'
```

```python
import ast

def safe_eval(x):
    """
    Safe evaluation of a string as a Python expression.

    Args:
        x (str): The input string to be evaluated.

    Returns:
        The result of the evaluated expression.

    Raises:
        ValueError: If the input string is not a valid Python literal.
    """

        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except (ValueError, SyntaxError):
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid expression: {x}")
```

```spl
-- Recipe 16: Reflection Agent
-- The agent solves a problem, then reflects on its own reasoning to catch errors.
-- If reflection finds issues, it corrects and re-reflects until confident.
-- Demonstrates meta-cognitive loops and self-assessment patterns.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="What are the trade-offs of microservices vs monoliths?"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a URL shortener system"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Explain why bubble sort is O(n^2)"

WORKFLOW reflection_agent
    INPUT: @problem TEXT
    OUTPUT: @answer TEXT
DO
    @iteration := 0
    @max_reflections := 3
    @confidence := 0

    -- Initial attempt at solving the problem
    GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer

    -- Reflection loop: assess and improve
    WHILE @iteration < @max_reflections DO
        -- Reflect: identify errors, gaps, or weak reasoning
        GENERATE reflect(@problem, @answer) INTO @reflection

        -- Score confidence in the answer
        GENERATE confidence_score(@answer, @reflection) INTO @confidence

        EVALUATE @confidence
            WHEN > 0.85 THEN
                COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'confident', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration
            OTHERWISE
                -- Extract specific issues to fix
                GENERATE extract_issues(@reflection) INTO @issues

                -- Correct the answer based on identified issues
                GENERATE correct(@answer, @issues, @problem) INTO @answer

                @iteration := @iteration + 1
        END
    END

    -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort with final confidence
    COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'best_effort', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'max_reflections', confidence = @confidence
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'restarted'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 13
     | Tokens: 14869 in / 10398 out
     | Latency: 157904ms

```output
To design a URL shortener system, we need to consider several factors such as scalability, security, and user experience. Here's a high-level overview of how you can design such a system:

### Overview

The URL shortener system will consist of the following components:

1.  **Database**: To store the mapping between original URLs and their shortened versions.
2.  **API Gateway**: To handle incoming requests and return responses.
3.  **Worker Service**: To generate shortened URLs and update the database.

### Database Design

We'll use a NoSQL database like MongoDB or Cassandra to store the URL mappings. The data will be stored in a JSON-like format for simplicity.

**url_mappings.json**
```json
{
    "urls": {
        "https://www.example.com/very-long-url-HERE",
        "https://www.example.com/another-long-url",
        ...
    }
}
```
     | We'll use a hash function to generate unique shortened URLs. For this example, we'll use the `uuid` library to create secure and random IDs.
     | 
     | ### Worker Service Design
     | 
     | The worker service will be responsible for generating shortened URLs and updating the database. We'll use Node.js and Express.js to build the service.
     | 
     | **worker-service.js**
```javascript
const express = require('express');
const app = express();
const uuid = require('uuid');

// Load the database from JSON file
const db = require('./url_mappings.json');

app.get('/shorten', (req, res) => {
    const originalUrl = req.query.url;
    if (!originalUrl) return res.status(400).send('URL is required');

    // Generate a shortened URL using UUID
    const shortUrl = `https://example.com/${uuid.v4()}`;

    // Validate and format the data
    const validatedUrlMap = validateAndFormatUrlMap(originalUrl, shortUrl);

    // Update the database with the new mapping
    db.save(validatedUrlMap, (error) => {
        if (error) {
            console.error('Error saving to database:', error);
            return res.status(500).send({ message: 'Error saving URL mapping' });
        }
        // ...
    });

    // Return the shortened URL
    res.json({ shortUrl });
});

app.listen(3000, () => {
    console.log('Worker service listening on port 3000');
});
```
     | ### API Gateway Design
     | 
     | The API gateway will handle incoming requests and return responses. We'll use Node.js and Express.js to build the gateway.
     | 
     | **api-gateway.js**
```javascript
const express = require('express');
const app = express();
const workerServiceUrl = 'http://localhost:3000';

app.get('/shorten', (req, res) => {
    const originalUrl = req.query.url;
    if (!originalUrl || typeof originalUrl !== 'string') return res.status(400).send('URL is required');

    // Send a request to the worker service to generate a shortened URL
    fetch(`${workerServiceUrl}/shorten?url=${originalUrl}`)
        .then(response => response.json())
        .then((data) => {
            const shortUrl = data.shortUrl;
            res.json({ shortUrl });
        })
        .catch(error => console.error(error));
});

app.listen(3001, () => {
    console.log('API gateway listening on port 3001');
});
```
     | ### Example Usage
     | 
     | To use the URL shortener system:
     | 
     | 1.  Send a GET request to `http://localhost:3001/shorten?url=https://www.example.com/very-long-url-HERE`.
     | 2.  The API gateway will send a request to the worker service to generate a shortened URL.
     | 3.  The worker service will return a JSON response with the shortened URL, which will be stored in the database.
     | 
     | ### Code Organization
     | 
     | The code can be organized into separate files and directories for better maintainability:
     | 
     | *   `url_mappings.json`: Stores the mapping between original URLs and their shortened versions.
     | *   `worker-service.js`: Handles incoming requests and generates shortened URLs.
     | *   `api-gateway.js`: Handles incoming requests and sends requests to the worker service.
     | 
     | This design provides a basic URL shortener system that can be extended and improved upon.
     | ```

     | **Database Design**

     | We'll use MongoDB as our database to store the mapping between original URLs and their shortened versions. We'll create a `UrlMap` model to interact with the database.
