     | **worker-service.js**
```javascript
const express = require('express');
const router = express.Router();
const mongoose = require('mongoose');
const urlMapSchema = new mongoose.Schema({
  originalUrl: String,
  shortUrl: String
});
const UrlMap = mongoose.model('UrlMap', urlMapSchema);
const uuid = require('uuid');

router.get('/shorten', async (req, res) => {
  if (!req.query.url || typeof req.query.url !== 'string') return res.status(400).send('URL is required');

  try {
    const shortUrl = uuid.v4();
    const validatedUrlMap = await validateAndFormatUrlMap(req.query.url, shortUrl);
    const urlMap = new UrlMap(validatedUrlMap);
    await urlMap.save();
    res.json({ shortUrl });
  } catch (error) {
    console.error(error);
    return res.status(500).send({ message: 'Error saving URL mapping' });
  }
});

module.exports = router;
```

```javascript
const { v4: uuidv4 } = require('uuid');

function validateAndFormatUrlMap(originalUrl, shortUrl) {
  const validatedUrlMap = {
    originalUrl,
    shortUrl
  };
  return validatedUrlMap;
}

module.exports = validateAndFormatUrlMap;

app.use(express.json());

app.get('/shorten', async (req, res) => {
  if (!req.query.url || typeof req.query.url !== 'string') return res.status(400).send('URL is required');

  try {
    const shortUrlResponse = await fetch(`${workerServiceUrl}/shorten?url=${req.query.url}`);
    const shortUrlData = await shortUrlResponse.json();

     |   @reflection = The provided code is a well-structured and readable implementation of a URL shortener system. However, there are some suggestions for improvement:
     | 
     | 1. Database Design:
     |    - Instead of using a simple JSON file as the database, consider using a proper database like MongoDB or PostgreSQL. This will allow you to store more data and scale better.
     |    - You can use a library like `mongoose` in Node.js to interact with MongoDB.
     | 
     | 2. Worker Service:
     |    - The worker service is not designed to handle multiple concurrent requests. Consider using an async/await approach or a promise-based method to handle this.
     |    - When generating the shortened URL, ensure that it's unique and stored in the database. To do this, you can use a UUID library like `uuid` or generate an alphanumeric ID.
     | 
     | 3. API Gateway:
     |    - Similar to the worker service, consider using async/await in the API gateway to handle concurrent requests.
     |    - Validate user input before sending it to the worker service. You can use libraries like `joi` for this purpose.
     | 
     | 4. Security:
     |    - Consider implementing rate limiting and IP blocking to prevent abuse of your URL shortener system.
     |    - If you're planning to expose your system to the public, ensure that sensitive data is not stored in plain text or sent via HTTP responses.
     | 
     | 5. Error Handling:
     |    - Implement a robust error handling mechanism throughout the application. This will help you catch and handle errors more efficiently.
     |    - Consider logging critical errors so you can track any issues with your system.
     | 
     | 6. Code Quality:
     |    - Use consistent naming conventions and comments to make the code easier to understand.
     |    - Follow node best practices (like using `const` instead of `var`) for better maintainability.
     |

```javascript
// worker-service.js

const express = require('express');
const router = express.Router();
const mongoose = require('mongoose');
const urlMapSchema = new mongoose.Schema({
    originalUrl: String,
    shortUrl: { type: String, unique: true },
});
const UrlMap = mongoose.model('UrlMap', urlMapSchema);

async function validateAndFormatUrlMap(originalUrl) {
    try {
        // Use UUID to generate a shortened URL that's unique
        const shortUrl = await generateShortUrl();
        return { originalUrl, shortUrl };
    } catch (error) {
        console.error(error);
        throw new Error('Error generating shortened URL');
    }
}

async function generateShortUrl() {
    const randomId = await generateRandomId();
    return `http://localhost:3000/${randomId}`;
}

router.get('/shorten', async (req, res) => {
    if (!req.query.url || typeof req.query.url !== 'string') return res.status(400).send('URL is required');

    try {
        const { originalUrl } = await validateAndFormatUrlMap(req.query.url);
        await UrlMap.create(originalUrl);
        res.json({ shortUrl: `http://localhost:3000/${originalUrl}` });
    } catch (error) {
        console.error(error);
        return res.status(500).send({ message: 'Error saving URL mapping' });
    }
});

module.exports = router;
```

// api-gateway.js

const express = require('express');
const app = express();
const workerServiceUrl = 'http://localhost:3000';
app.use(express.json());

async function validateAndFormatUrlMap(originalUrl) {
    try {
        // Use UUID to generate a shortened URL that's unique
        const shortUrl = await generateShortUrl();
        return { originalUrl, shortUrl };
    } catch (error) {
        console.error(error);
        throw new Error('Error generating shortened URL');
    }
}

async function generateShortUrl() {
    const randomId = await generateRandomId();
    return `http://localhost:3000/${randomId}`;
}

app.get('/shorten', async (req, res) => {
    if (!req.query.url || typeof req.query.url !== 'string') return res.status(400).send('URL is required');

    try {
        const shortUrlResponse = await fetch(`${workerServiceUrl}/shorten?url=${req.query.url}`);
        const shortUrlData = await shortUrlResponse.json();
        res.json({ shortUrl: `http://localhost:3000/${shortUrlData.originalUrl}` });
    } catch (error) {
        console.error(error);
        return res.status(500).send({ message: 'Error sending request to worker service' });
    }
});

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 17: Tree of Thought
-- Explores multiple reasoning paths in parallel, evaluates each, and selects the best.
-- Demonstrates branching exploration, comparative evaluation, and path scoring.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="How should a startup prioritize between growth and profitability?"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a caching strategy for a high-traffic API"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Should we rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor?"

WORKFLOW tree_of_thought
    INPUT: @problem TEXT
    OUTPUT: @best_solution TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Generate multiple initial approaches
    GENERATE approach_1(@problem) INTO @path_a
    GENERATE approach_2(@problem) INTO @path_b
    GENERATE approach_3(@problem) INTO @path_c

    -- Step 2: Develop each approach one level deeper
    GENERATE develop(@path_a, @problem) INTO @path_a_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_b, @problem) INTO @path_b_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_c, @problem) INTO @path_c_developed

    -- Step 3: Score each path on multiple criteria
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_a_developed, @problem) INTO @score_a
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_b_developed, @problem) INTO @score_b
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_c_developed, @problem) INTO @score_c

    -- Step 4: Select the best path
    GENERATE select_best(
        @path_a_developed, @score_a,
        @path_b_developed, @score_b,
        @path_c_developed, @score_c
    ) INTO @best_path

    -- Step 5: Refine the winning path into a final solution
    GENERATE refine_solution(@best_path, @problem) INTO @best_solution

    -- Step 6: Verify the solution holds up
    GENERATE verify(@best_solution, @problem) INTO @verification

    EVALUATE @verification
        WHEN 'sound' THEN
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'complete', paths_explored = 3
        OTHERWISE
            -- If verification fails, combine insights from all paths
            GENERATE synthesize_all(
                @path_a_developed, @path_b_developed, @path_c_developed, @problem
            ) INTO @best_solution
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'synthesized', paths_explored = 3
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- Return whatever path was scored highest so far
        COMMIT @best_path WITH status = 'budget_limit'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END
```

```output
Based on the inputs provided, I recommend adopting an **Incremental Refactoring** approach for updating your legacy system. This approach balances progress with risk management and offers a more sustainable solution that maintains the overall structure and functionality of the system.

The key takeaways from the inputs are:

1.  **Less risk**: Incremental refactoring reduces the overall complexity and risk associated with rewriting the entire legacy system.
2.  **More manageable workload**: By breaking down the work into smaller, independent tasks, you can manage your workloads more effectively and avoid feeling overwhelmed.
3.  **Faster feedback loop**: This approach enables quick testing and validation of each refactored component, allowing for faster iteration and improvement.
4.  **Maintainability**: Incremental refactoring helps maintain the overall structure and functionality of the legacy system.

To proceed with incremental refactoring, it's essential to:

1.  Identify areas of the legacy system that need improvement or refactoring.
2.  Break down the work into smaller increments (e.g., weeks, months).
3.  Develop a plan for each increment, including specific goals, tasks, and timelines.
4.  Refactor the identified areas in each increment while maintaining backward compatibility.

By adopting an incremental refactoring approach, you can ensure that your changes are well-planned, carefully executed, and aligned with the needs of your organization.

Rewriting the entire legacy system may not be the best option due to the potential risks and disruptions it could cause. Instead, incremental refactoring allows for a more controlled and sustainable approach to updating the system.

Incremental refactoring enables you to:

*   Focus on specific areas of improvement or refactoring without disrupting the entire system.
*   Develop and test new features incrementally, allowing for faster feedback and iteration.
*   Manage your workload more effectively by breaking down the work into smaller tasks.
*   Maintain the overall structure and functionality of the legacy system while making improvements.

By adopting an incremental refactoring approach, you can ensure that your changes are well-planned, carefully executed, and aligned with the needs of your organization.

```spl
-- Recipe 18: Guardrails Pipeline
-- Input validation → safe generation → output validation → response.
-- Demonstrates safety-first patterns: content filtering, PII detection, and output sanitization.
-- Four gates before a response is committed.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=safe_encryption
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=pii_ssn
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=harmful_malware
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       user_input="My SSN is 123-45-6789, help me file taxes"
--
-- Discover available test inputs:
--   spl run ... input_id=list

WORKFLOW guardrails_pipeline
    INPUT:  @input_id   TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @user_input TEXT DEFAULT ''
    OUTPUT: @safe_response TEXT
    SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
    -- Step 1: Load test input from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- If input_id is blank, @user_input is used directly in subsequent steps.
    CALL load_test_input(@input_id) INTO @test_context

    -- Gate 1a: Keyword pre-screen (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Fast check for obvious harmful / off-topic patterns before spending tokens.
    CALL classify_input_keywords(@user_input) INTO @keyword_class

    EVALUATE @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        OTHERWISE
            -- Continue to LLM classification
    END

    -- Gate 1b: LLM input content classification (nuanced cases keywords miss)
    GENERATE classify_input(@user_input, @test_context) INTO @input_class

    EVALUATE @input_class
        WHEN 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        WHEN 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        OTHERWISE
            -- Input is acceptable, continue
    END

    -- Gate 2: PII detection (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL detect_pii(@user_input) INTO @pii_report

    EVALUATE @pii_report
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'pii_found' THEN
            -- Gate 2b: Redact PII (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL redact_pii(@user_input) INTO @clean_input
        OTHERWISE
            SET @clean_input = @user_input
    END

    -- Gate 3: Generate response with safety-tuned prompt
    GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @raw_response

    -- Gate 4: Output validation — check for PII leakage or harmful content
    GENERATE validate_output(@raw_response, @clean_input) INTO @output_check

    EVALUATE @output_check
        WHEN 'safe' THEN
            SET @safe_response = @raw_response
        WHEN 'contains_pii' THEN
            -- Deterministic PII redaction on the output too
            CALL redact_pii(@raw_response) INTO @safe_response
        WHEN 'hallucination' THEN
            GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @safe_response
            RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
        OTHERWISE
            SET @safe_response = 'I was unable to generate a safe response. Please rephrase your question.'
    END

    COMMIT @safe_response WITH
        status       = 'complete',
        input_class  = @input_class,
        pii_detected = @pii_report

EXCEPTION
    WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
        COMMIT 'This request cannot be processed.' WITH status = 'refused'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        COMMIT 'Unable to provide a verified response.' WITH status = 'hallucination_blocked'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @safe_response WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

```output
**Encryption Explained**

Encryption is the process of converting plaintext (readable data) into ciphertext (unreadable data) to protect it from unauthorized access. Here's a simplified overview of how encryption works:

1. **Key Generation**: A key pair is generated, consisting of a public key and a private key. The public key is used for encryption, while the private key is used for decryption.
2. **Encryption Algorithm**: An encryption algorithm (such as AES or RSA) is applied to the plaintext data using the public key. This process transforms the plaintext into ciphertext.
3. **Ciphertext Output**: The resulting ciphertext is the encrypted data that can only be decrypted with the corresponding private key.
4. **Decryption**: When the recipient has access to the private key, they can apply the same encryption algorithm to the ciphertext, using their private key as input.

**Encryption Types**

There are several types of encryption:

1. **Symmetric Encryption**: Uses the same key for both encryption and decryption (e.g., AES).
2. **Asymmetric Encryption**: Uses a pair of keys: one public key for encryption and a corresponding private key for decryption (e.g., RSA).

**Why is Encryption Important?**

Encryption provides several benefits:

1. **Confidentiality**: Protects data from unauthorized access.
2. **Data Integrity**: Ensures that data remains unmodified during transmission or storage.
3. **Authentication**: Verifies the identity of the sender and receiver.

By using encryption, individuals and organizations can safeguard sensitive information, ensuring confidentiality, integrity, and authenticity of their data.

Would you like to know more about a specific aspect of encryption?

     |   @keyword_class = **Classifying Input Keywords using Natural Language Processing (NLP)**
     | 
     | The `classify_input_keywords` procedure is a text classification technique used to categorize input keywords into predefined categories. In this example, we'll use the `explain how encryption works` string as input.
     | 
     | **Step 1: Tokenization**
     | 
     | Tokenization involves breaking down the input string into individual words or tokens. We can use the NLTK library in Python to perform tokenization.
```python
import nltk
from nltk.tokenize import word_tokenize

input_string = "explain how encryption works"
tokens = word_tokenize(input_string)
print(tokens)  # Output: ['explain', 'how', 'encryption', 'works']
```
     | **Step 2: Stopword Removal**
     | 
     | Stopwords are common words like "the," "and," etc. that do not carry much meaning in text classification. We can use the NLTK library to remove stopwords from our tokens.
```python
from nltk.corpus import stopwords

stop_words = set(stopwords.words('english'))
filtered_tokens = [token for token in tokens if token.lower() not in stop_words]
print(filtered_tokens)  # Output: ['explain', 'how', 'encryption', 'works']
```
     | **Step 3: Stemming or Lemmatization**
     | 
     | Stemming or lemmatization reduces words to their base form, making it easier for our algorithm to compare them. We can use the PorterStemmer class from NLTK to perform stemming.
```python
from nltk.stem import PorterStemmer

stemmer = PorterStemmer()
stemmed_tokens = [stemmer.stem(token) for token in filtered_tokens]
print(stemmed_tokens)  # Output: ['explain', 'how', 'encrypt', 'work']
```
     | **Step 4: Bag-of-Words Representation**
     | 
     | A bag-of-words representation represents our input text as a vector of word frequencies. We can use the CountVectorizer class from scikit-learn to create this representation.
```python
from sklearn.feature_extraction.text import CountVectorizer

vectorizer = CountVectorizer()
X = vectorizer.fit_transform([' '.join(stemmed_tokens)])
print(X.toarray())  # Output: [[1. 0. 0. 0.]]
```
     | **Step 5: Classification**
     | 
     | We can train a classifier using the bag-of-words representation and our predefined categories.
     | 
     | **Example Code**
```python
import nltk
from nltk.tokenize import word_tokenize
from nltk.corpus import stopwords
from nltk.stem import PorterStemmer
from sklearn.feature_extraction.text import CountVectorizer
from sklearn.naive_bayes import MultinomialNB

# Preprocessing steps
nltk.download('punkt')
nltk.download('stopwords')

input_string = "explain how encryption works"
tokens = word_tokenize(input_string)
stop_words = set(stopwords.words('english'))
filtered_tokens = [token for token in tokens if token.lower() not in stop_words]
stemmer = PorterStemmer()
stemmed_tokens = [stemmer.stem(token) for token in filtered_tokens]

# Bag-of-words representation
vectorizer = CountVectorizer()
X = vectorizer.fit_transform([' '.join(stemmed_tokens)])

# Classification using Naive Bayes classifier
clf = MultinomialNB()
clf.fit(X, ['classification'])
```

     |   @pii_report = Encryption is a process of converting plaintext data into unreadable ciphertext to protect it from unauthorized access. Here's an explanation on how encryption works and the `detect_pii` procedure:
     | 
     | **How Encryption Works:**
     | 
     | 1. **Key Generation**: A key is generated, which is used for both encryption and decryption.
     | 2. **Encryption Process**: The plaintext data is converted into ciphertext using a cryptographic algorithm (e.g., AES). The key is used to determine the transformation of each plaintext byte into the corresponding ciphertext byte.
     | 3. **Decryption Process**: To decrypt the ciphertext, the same key is used to reverse the transformation, resulting in the original plaintext.
     | 
     | **detect_pii Procedure:**
     | 
     | The `detect_pii` procedure is designed to detect potentially identifiable information (PII) in a given dataset. Here's an explanation of how it might work:
     | 
     | 1. **Data Preprocessing**: The input data is cleaned and preprocessed to remove any irrelevant or unnecessary information.
     | 2. **Feature Extraction**: Relevant features are extracted from the data, such as names, addresses, phone numbers, etc.
     | 3. **Pattern Matching**: The extracted features are matched against a database of known PII patterns using machine learning algorithms (e.g., neural networks).
     | 4. **Classification**: The output is classified into either "PII detected" or "no PII detected".
     | 5. **Report Generation**: A report is generated indicating whether any PII was detected and if so, what specific information was found.
     | 
     | **Example Code:**
     | 
     | Here's a simplified example of how the `detect_pii` procedure might be implemented in Python using machine learning:
```python
import pandas as pd
from sklearn.feature_extraction.text import TfidfVectorizer
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split
from sklearn.ensemble import RandomForestClassifier

# Load dataset
df = pd.read_csv("data.csv")

# Preprocess data
df["name"] = df["name"].str.lower()
df["address"] = df["address"].str.replace("\n", " ")
df["phone_number"] = df["phone_number"].str.replace("-", "")

# Extract features
vectorizer = TfidfVectorizer(stop_words="english")
X = vectorizer.fit_transform([" ".join(df["name"]), " ".join(df["address"]), " ".join(df["phone_number"])])
y = pd.Series([0, 1, 1, 1] * 10)  # labels for PII detection

# Split data into training and testing sets
X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(X, y, test_size=0.2)

# Train model
model = RandomForestClassifier(n_estimators=100)
model.fit(X_train, y_train)

# Make predictions on test set
y_pred = model.predict(X_test)

# Evaluate model performance
accuracy = model.score(X_test, y_test)
print(f"Accuracy: {accuracy:.3f}")

# Detect PII in new data
new_data = ["John Doe 123 Main St", "Jane Smith 456 Elm St"]
X_new = vectorizer.transform([" ".join(new_data[0]), " ".join(new_data[1])])
y_pred_new = model.predict(X_new)
if y_pred_new[0]:
    print("PII detected!")
else:
    print("No PII detected.")
```

     |

     | 2. **Encryption Algorithm**: An encryption algorithm (such as AES or RSA) is applied to the plaintext data using the public key. This process transforms the plaintext into ciphertext.
     | 3. **Ciphertext Output**: The resulting ciphertext is the encrypted data that can only be decrypted with the corresponding private key.
     | 4. **Decryption**: When the recipient has access to the private key, they can apply the same encryption algorithm to the ciphertext, using their private key as input.
     | 
     | **Key Concepts**
     | 
     | *   **Symmetric Encryption**: Uses the same key for both encryption and decryption (e.g., AES).
     | *   **Asymmetric Encryption**: Uses a pair of keys: one public key for encryption and a corresponding private key for decryption (e.g., RSA).

```spl
-- Recipe 19: Memory-Augmented Conversation
-- A conversational agent that remembers facts from prior turns using persistent memory.
-- Demonstrates memory.get/memory.set, context accumulation, and stateful interactions.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="My name is Alice and I'm a data scientist"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="What's my name?"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="I prefer Python over R"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="What do you know about me?"
--
-- Memory persists across runs via `spl memory` store.
-- Reset with: spl memory delete chat_user_profile

WORKFLOW memory_conversation
    INPUT: @user_input TEXT
    OUTPUT: @response TEXT
DO
    -- Load existing user profile from memory
    SELECT memory.get('chat_user_profile') AS profile INTO @profile
    SELECT memory.get('chat_history') AS chat_history INTO @chat_history

    -- Extract any new facts the user shared about themselves
    GENERATE extract_facts(@user_input) INTO @new_facts

    -- Update profile with new facts (if any)
    EVALUATE @new_facts
        WHEN 'no_new_facts' THEN
            -- Nothing to update
        OTHERWISE
            GENERATE merge_profile(@profile, @new_facts) INTO @profile
            -- STORE @profile IN memory.chat_user_profile  -- workflow-level memory write not yet supported
    END

    -- Generate response with full context: profile + history + current input
    GENERATE contextual_reply(@user_input, @profile, @chat_history) INTO @response

    -- Update chat history (keep last 10 turns)
    @chat_history := @chat_history + '\nUser: ' + @user_input + '\nAssistant: ' + @response
    GENERATE trim_history(@chat_history, 10) INTO @chat_history
    -- STORE @chat_history IN memory.chat_history  -- workflow-level memory write not yet supported

    COMMIT @response WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT 'I remember you! But I ran out of budget for this response.' WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

```spl
-- Recipe 20: Ensemble Voting
-- Generates multiple independent answers, then uses majority voting to pick the best.
-- Demonstrates ensemble methods, comparative scoring, and consensus-driven output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 question="What causes inflation?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="Is Rust faster than C++?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the best database for time-series data?"

WORKFLOW ensemble_voting
    INPUT: @question TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_answer TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Generate 5 independent candidate answers with varying temperatures
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_1
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_2
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_3
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_4
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_5

    -- Step 2: Score each candidate on accuracy, completeness, and clarity
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_1, @question) INTO @score_1
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_2, @question) INTO @score_2
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_3, @question) INTO @score_3
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_4, @question) INTO @score_4
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_5, @question) INTO @score_5

    -- Step 3: Find consensus — identify common themes across candidates
    GENERATE find_consensus(
        @candidate_1, @candidate_2, @candidate_3,
        @candidate_4, @candidate_5
    ) INTO @consensus

    -- Step 4: Select the best candidate based on scores and consensus alignment
    GENERATE select_winner(
        @candidate_1, @score_1,
        @candidate_2, @score_2,
        @candidate_3, @score_3,
        @candidate_4, @score_4,
        @candidate_5, @score_5,
        @consensus
    ) INTO @best_candidate

    -- Step 5: Polish the winning answer, incorporating consensus insights
    GENERATE polish(@best_candidate, @consensus, @question) INTO @final_answer

    COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'complete', candidates = 5

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- If budget runs out, use whatever candidates we have
        GENERATE select_winner(
            @candidate_1, @score_1,
            @candidate_2, @score_2,
            @candidate_3, @score_3
        ) INTO @final_answer
        COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'partial', candidates = 3
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END

```python
import random

def score_candidate():
    # Define the possible answers and their corresponding scores
    possible_answers = [
        ("Monetary policy", 1),
        ("Demand-pull inflation", 1),
        ("Cost-push inflation", 1),
        ("Supply chain disruptions", 1),
        ("Economic growth", 1),
        ("Fiscal policy", 1),
        ("Commodity price shocks", 1)
    ]

    # Initialize the candidate score to 0
    candidate_score = 0

    # Check if the input list is empty or contains duplicate answers
    if len(possible_answers) == 0:
        raise ValueError("Input list cannot be empty")
    elif len(set([answer for answer, _ in possible_answers])) != len(possible_answers):
        raise ValueError("Input list must contain unique answers")

    # Shuffle the possible answers using a more robust method
    random.shuffle(possible_answers)

    # Iterate over the shuffled possible answers and check if each answer is correct
    for answer, score in possible_answers:
        if answer == possible_answers[0]:
            candidate_score += score

    return candidate_score
```

```spl
-- Recipe 21: Multi-Model Pipeline
-- Showcases GENERATE ... USING MODEL per-step model selection
-- and semantic WHILE loop with variable context.
--
-- Each step can target a different model — the right model for each task.
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic="climate change"

CREATE FUNCTION research(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a research specialist.
Provide key facts, statistics, and recent developments about: {topic}
Focus on factual accuracy. Be thorough but concise.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION analyze(facts TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a data analyst.
Analyze the following research and identify the 3 most significant insights:

{facts}

For each insight, explain why it matters and rate its significance (1-10).
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION write_summary(analysis TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a professional writer.
Write a clear, engaging 2-paragraph summary based on this analysis:

{analysis}

The first paragraph should present the key findings.
The second paragraph should discuss implications and outlook.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION quality_check(text TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a quality reviewer.
Rate the following text on a scale of 0.0 to 1.0 for:
- Clarity
- Accuracy
- Completeness

Return ONLY a single number (the average score).

Text: {text}
$$;

WORKFLOW multi_model_pipeline
  INPUT: @topic TEXT
  OUTPUT: @final TEXT
DO
  -- Step 1: Research with a fast model (good for factual retrieval)
  GENERATE research(@topic) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @facts;

  -- Step 2: Analysis with a reasoning model
  GENERATE analyze(@facts) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @analysis;

  -- Step 3: Writing with a creative model
  GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @draft;

  -- Step 4: Quality loop — refine until score > 0.7 (max 3 iterations)
  @iteration := 0;
  @quality := 0;

  WHILE @iteration < 3 DO
    GENERATE quality_check(@draft) INTO @quality;

    EVALUATE @quality
      WHEN > 0.7 THEN
        COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'high_quality', score = @quality
      OTHERWISE
        GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) INTO @draft
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
    END
  END

  -- Fallback: commit whatever we have
  COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'max_iterations', score = @quality

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN ModelOverloaded THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'model_overloaded'
END;
```

```spl
-- Recipe 23: Structured Output
-- Extract typed, structured data from free-form text using CREATE FUNCTION with JSON schema.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained generation — the LLM must return valid JSON matching the spec.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="John Smith, 42, joined Acme Corp in March 2021 earning $95,000/year"
--
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="Invoice #INV-2045 from TechSupplies Ltd dated 15 Jan 2024 for $3,420.50 due in 30 days"

-- Define the extraction schema
CREATE FUNCTION extract_entity_schema()
RETURNS JSON AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "people": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":       { "type": "string" },
          "age":        { "type": "integer" },
          "role":       { "type": "string" },
          "salary":     { "type": "number" },
          "start_date": { "type": "string", "format": "date" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "organizations": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":     { "type": "string" },
          "type":     { "type": "string" },
          "industry": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "amounts": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value":    { "type": "number" },
          "currency": { "type": "string", "default": "USD" },
          "label":    { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    },
    "dates": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value": { "type": "string" },
          "label": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    }
  }
}
$$;

PROMPT extract_entities
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise data extraction engine. Extract all entities from the text and return valid JSON matching the provided schema. Return only the JSON object, no explanation.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extract_entity_schema() AS schema
GENERATE structured_extraction(text, schema)

```spl
-- Recipe 24: Few-Shot Prompting
-- Embed gold-standard examples directly in the SELECT context to guide output format and style.
-- Demonstrates in-context learning — no fine-tuning required.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="The quarterly results exceeded all analyst forecasts by a significant margin"
--
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="System outage detected in EU-WEST-2 affecting 15% of users" \
--       domain="ops"

-- Gold-standard examples for tone/format calibration
CREATE FUNCTION few_shot_examples(domain TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE domain
  WHEN 'finance' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Revenue grew 12% YoY despite headwinds in APAC."
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.7, "topics": ["revenue", "growth", "APAC"], "summary": "Revenue up 12% YoY."}

Input: "Write-downs of $2.3B drove a net loss for Q3."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.9, "topics": ["write-downs", "net loss", "Q3"], "summary": "Q3 net loss from $2.3B write-downs."}'

  WHEN 'ops' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Deployment succeeded. All health checks passing. P99 latency 45ms."
Output: {"status": "healthy", "severity": "none", "action_required": false, "summary": "Deploy OK, latency nominal."}

Input: "Pod crash-loop in prod-east. OOMKilled. Restarted 7 times in 10 min."
Output: {"status": "incident", "severity": "high", "action_required": true, "summary": "OOM crash-loop in prod-east, immediate attention needed."}'

  ELSE
    '### Examples

Input: "The new feature launched today and users are loving it!"
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.8, "summary": "Successful feature launch with positive user reception."}

Input: "We encountered several critical bugs that delayed the release by two weeks."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.7, "summary": "Critical bugs caused two-week release delay."}'
END
$$;

PROMPT few_shot_classifier
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise text classifier. Follow the examples exactly — same JSON structure, same field names. Return only the JSON object.'),
    few_shot_examples(context.domain) AS examples,
    context.text AS text,
    context.domain AS domain
GENERATE classify(text, examples)

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 25: Nested Procedures
-- Demonstrates PROCEDURE calling PROCEDURE — deep composability without external orchestration.
-- Pattern: a high-level workflow delegates subtasks to reusable PROCEDURE units.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       topic="quantum computing" audience="high school students"
--
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama \
--       topic="CRISPR gene editing" audience="policy makers" depth="deep"

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a single explanation layer
PROCEDURE explain_layer(content TEXT, audience TEXT, style TEXT DEFAULT 'clear and engaging')
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE explain(content, audience, style) INTO @explanation
    COMMIT @explanation
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a concrete example for a concept
PROCEDURE make_example(concept TEXT, context TEXT, audience TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE concrete_example(concept, context, audience) INTO @example
    COMMIT @example
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: check comprehension level and simplify if needed
PROCEDURE calibrate_complexity(text TEXT, audience TEXT, target_grade INT DEFAULT 8)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE assess_reading_level(text) INTO @reading_level
    EVALUATE @reading_level
        WHEN > target_grade THEN
            GENERATE simplify(text, audience) INTO @calibrated
            COMMIT @calibrated
        OTHERWISE
            COMMIT text
    END
END

-- Outer WORKFLOW: orchestrates the inner procedures
WORKFLOW layered_explainer
    INPUT: @topic TEXT, @audience TEXT, @depth TEXT DEFAULT 'standard'
    OUTPUT: @article TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research the topic
    GENERATE research_overview(@topic) INTO @overview

    -- Step 2: Delegate explanation to inner procedure
    CALL explain_layer(
        content  = @overview,
        audience = @audience,
        style    = 'clear, engaging, avoid jargon'
    ) INTO @base_explanation

    -- Step 3: Generate a concrete example via another inner procedure
    CALL make_example(
        concept  = @topic,
        context  = @base_explanation,
        audience = @audience
    ) INTO @example

    -- Step 4: Calibrate complexity (nested call inside a conditional)
    CALL calibrate_complexity(
        text         = @base_explanation,
        audience     = @audience,
        target_grade = 8
    ) INTO @calibrated_explanation

    -- Step 5: Assemble the final article
    GENERATE assemble_article(
        @topic,
        @calibrated_explanation,
        @example,
        @depth
    ) INTO @article

    COMMIT @article WITH status = 'complete', audience = @audience
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 5
     | Tokens: 2755 in / 2356 out
     | Latency: 31638ms

```output
Here's the assembled article:

**What is Quantum Computing?**

Imagine you have a super powerful computer that can solve problems much faster than the computers we use today. That's basically what quantum computing is.

**Classical Computers vs Quantum Computers**

Regular computers, like your phone or laptop, use "bits" to store information. Bits are either 0 or 1, and they work like on/off switches. But quantum computers use something called "qubits," which can be both 0 and 1 at the same time! This means that qubits can process a lot of information much faster than bits.

**How Qubits Work**

Qubits are special because they can exist in many states simultaneously, kind of like spinning tops. When you measure a qubit, it "collapses" into one specific state, which is like choosing which number the top was spinning at.

**Why Quantum Computing Matters**

Quantum computing has the potential to solve some really complex problems that are difficult or impossible for classical computers to solve. Here are a few examples:

* **Cryptography**: Quantum computers can break some encryption methods, but they can also be used to create new, unbreakable ones.
* **Optimization**: Quantum computers can quickly find the best solution to complex problems in fields like logistics and finance.
* **Simulation**: Quantum computers can simulate the behavior of tiny particles and molecules, which could lead to breakthroughs in fields like chemistry and materials science.

**The Challenges Ahead**

Quantum computing is still a relatively new field, and there are some challenges to overcome. For example:

* **Error Correction**: Qubits are fragile, so we need to find ways to correct errors when they happen.
* **Scalability**: Currently, most quantum computers are small-scale and require special resources to operate.

**The Future of Quantum Computing**

Quantum computing has the potential to revolutionize many fields, from medicine and finance to climate modeling and more. To achieve widespread adoption, we need to overcome some of the current challenges and develop new technologies that can harness the power of qubits.

So, what does this mean for you? Quantum computing is an exciting field that could lead to breakthroughs in many areas. Who knows, maybe one day you'll be a part of developing the next big thing in quantum computing!
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @topic = quantum computing
     |   @audience = high school students
     |   @depth = standard
     |   @overview = **Research Overview: Quantum Computing**
     | 
     | Quantum computing is a revolutionary technology that uses the principles of quantum mechanics to perform calculations and operations on data. It has the potential to solve complex problems that are currently unsolvable or require an unfeasible amount of time to solve using classical computers.
     | 
     | **Key Principles:**
     | 
     | 1. **Superposition**: Quantum bits (qubits) can exist in multiple states simultaneously, allowing for exponential scaling of computational power.
     | 2. **Entanglement**: Qubits can be connected in a way that allows them to affect each other, even when separated by large distances.
     | 3. **Quantum Measurement**: The act of measuring a qubit causes it to collapse into one specific state.
     | 
     | **Applications:**
     | 
     | 1. **Cryptography**: Quantum computers can break many classical encryption algorithms, but they can also be used to create unbreakable quantum encryption methods.
     | 2. **Optimization**: Quantum computers can quickly solve complex optimization problems, which has applications in fields such as logistics and finance.
     | 3. **Simulation**: Quantum computers can simulate the behavior of complex systems, allowing for breakthroughs in fields such as chemistry and materials science.
     | 
     | **Current Challenges:**
     | 
     | 1. **Error Correction**: Quantum computers are prone to errors due to the fragile nature of qubits, which must be corrected to maintain the integrity of computations.
     | 2. **Scalability**: Currently, most quantum computers are small-scale and require significant resources to operate.
     | 3. **Quantum Control**: Maintaining control over qubits is essential for reliable computation, but it can be difficult due to the noise inherent in quantum systems.
     | 
     | **Recent Developments:**
     | 
     | 1. **Google's Quantum AI Lab**: Google has announced a 72-qubit quantum computer that can perform complex calculations.
     | 2. **IBM Quantum Experience**: IBM offers a cloud-based quantum computing platform with a 53-qubit quantum processor.
     | 3. **Rigetti Computing**: Rigetti is developing a cloud-based quantum computing platform with up to 128 qubits.
     | 
     | **Future Directions:**
     | 
     | 1. **Quantum Error Correction**: Developing robust methods for error correction will be crucial for large-scale, reliable quantum computing.
     | 2. **Quantum Materials Science**: Researching new materials and technologies that can enhance the performance of quantum computers is an active area of study.
     | 3. **Quantum-Inspired Algorithms**: Developing algorithms that take advantage of quantum principles to solve complex problems in classical domains will be essential for practical applications.
     | 
     | **Conclusion:**
     | 
     | Quantum computing has the potential to revolutionize many fields, but significant research and development are needed to overcome current challenges and achieve widespread adoption.
     |   @base_explanation = Let's explain quantum computing in a way that's easy to understand for high school students:
     | 
     | **What is Quantum Computing?**

     | Imagine you have a super powerful computer that can solve problems much faster than the computers we use today. That's basically what quantum computing is.
     | 
     | **How Does it Work?**
     | 
     | Classical computers, like your phone or laptop, use "bits" to store information. Bits are either 0 or 1, and they work like on/off switches. But quantum computers use something called "qubits," which can be both 0 and 1 at the same time! This means that qubits can process a lot of information much faster than bits.
     | 
     | **What's So Special About Qubits?**
     | 
     | Qubits are special because they can exist in many states simultaneously, kind of like spinning tops. When you measure a qubit, it "collapses" into one specific state, which is like choosing which number the top was spinning at.
     | 
     | **How Can Quantum Computing Help Us?**
     | 
     | Quantum computing has the potential to solve some really complex problems that are difficult or impossible for classical computers to solve. Here are a few examples:
     | 
     | * **Cryptography**: Quantum computers can break some encryption methods, but they can also be used to create new, unbreakable ones.
     | * **Optimization**: Quantum computers can quickly find the best solution to complex problems in fields like logistics and finance.
     | * **Simulation**: Quantum computers can simulate the behavior of tiny particles and molecules, which could lead to breakthroughs in fields like chemistry and materials science.
     | 
     | **What's the Catch?**
     | 
     | Quantum computing is still a relatively new field, and there are some challenges to overcome. For example:
     | 
     | * **Error Correction**: Qubits are fragile, so we need to find ways to correct errors when they happen.
     | * **Scalability**: Currently, most quantum computers are small-scale and require special resources to operate.
     | 
     | **What's Happening Now?**
     | 
     | There are many companies and research institutions working on developing quantum computing technology. Some examples include:
     | 
     | * Google's Quantum AI Lab
     | * IBM Quantum Experience
     | * Rigetti Computing
     | 
     | These organizations are making progress, but there's still a lot of work to be done to make quantum computing practical for everyday use.
     | 
     | **What Does the Future Hold?**
     | 
     | Quantum computing has the potential to revolutionize many fields, from medicine and finance to climate modeling and more. To achieve widespread adoption, we need to overcome some of the current challenges and develop new technologies that can harness the power of qubits.

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 26: Prompt A/B Test
-- Compare two prompt variants using EVALUATE scoring. Pick the winner automatically.
-- Demonstrates data-driven prompt engineering built into the workflow itself.
-- Supports pre-built experiments from experiments.json or ad-hoc task + prompt_a + prompt_b.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=neural_networks
--
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=standing_desk winner_threshold=2.0
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       task="Explain neural networks" \
--       prompt_a="Explain like I'm 5" \
--       prompt_b="Give a technical explanation with analogies"
--
-- Discover available experiments:
--   spl run ... experiment_id=list

-- Scoring rubric as a reusable function
CREATE FUNCTION scoring_rubric()
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Rate the response on a scale of 0–10 for each criterion:
1. Clarity (0-10): Is it easy to understand?
2. Completeness (0-10): Does it fully address the task?
3. Relevance (0-10): Is every sentence on-topic?
4. Engagement (0-10): Is it interesting to read?

Return valid JSON only — no prose outside the JSON:
{"clarity": N, "completeness": N, "relevance": N, "engagement": N, "total": N, "rationale": "one concise sentence"}
$$;

WORKFLOW ab_test
    INPUT:  @experiment_id    TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @task             TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_a         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_b         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @winner_threshold FLOAT DEFAULT 1.5
    OUTPUT: @winner_output TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Load experiment from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Sets @experiment_context with task, prompt_a, prompt_b, and notes.
    -- If experiment_id is blank, the context will be empty and @task/@prompt_a/@prompt_b are used directly.
    CALL load_experiment(@experiment_id) INTO @experiment_context

    -- Step 2: Generate Variant A response
    GENERATE run_variant_a(
        @task,
        @prompt_a,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_a

    -- Step 3: Generate Variant B response
    GENERATE run_variant_b(
        @task,
        @prompt_b,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_b

    -- Step 4: Score both variants against the rubric (LLM)
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_a, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_a_json
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_b, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_b_json

    -- Step 5: Extract numeric totals (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_a_json) INTO @score_a
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_b_json) INTO @score_b

    -- Step 6: Pick winner or declare tie
    EVALUATE (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN > @winner_threshold THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_a
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'A',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN < (0 - @winner_threshold) THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_b
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'B',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_b - @score_a)
        OTHERWISE
            -- Scores too close — format both with explanation (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL format_tie_result(@response_a, @response_b, @score_a_json, @score_b_json) INTO @winner_output
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner  = 'tie',
                score_a = @score_a,
                score_b = @score_b
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'A/B test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

```output
To evaluate the "Evaluate Response" task, I'll break down what it could mean:

1.  **Understanding the Task**: The task involves evaluating the quality or effectiveness of a response to a particular question, prompt, or scenario.
2.  **Criteria for Evaluation**: To effectively evaluate a response, consider factors such as:
    *   Relevance: How well does the response address the question or prompt?
    *   Accuracy: Does the response provide accurate information or insights?
    *   Clarity: Is the response clear and easy to understand?
    *   Completeness: Does the response cover all necessary points or aspects of the topic?
3.  **Evaluation Rubric**: Establish a scoring rubric that assesses these criteria and provides a clear framework for evaluation.

Here's an example of how you might approach this task:

```python
def evaluate_response(response, prompt):
    # Define evaluation criteria
    criteria = {
        "relevance": lambda x: len(x.split()) > 10,
        "accuracy": lambda x: all(word in response for word in ["good", "great", "excellent"]),
        "clarity": lambda x: any(char.isalpha() for char in response),
        "completeness": lambda x: len(response) > 50
    }

    # Evaluate the response against each criterion and calculate scores
    scores = {criterion_name: criterion(criterion_func(response)) for criterion_name, criterion_func in criteria.items()}

    return scores

# Example usage:
prompt = "What are the benefits of neural networks?"
response = "Neural networks have numerous benefits, including improved accuracy, efficiency, and scalability."

scores = evaluate_response(response, prompt)
print(scores)
```

     |     *   **Artificial Neural Networks (ANNs)**: ANNs are designed to mimic the human brain's structure and function. They consist of interconnected nodes or "neurons," which process inputs and produce outputs based on complex algorithms.
     |     *   **Types of Neural Networks**: There are several types, including:
     |         *   **Feedforward Networks**: Information flows only in one direction, from input layer to output layer.
     |         *   **Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs)**: Feedback connections allow information to flow in a loop, enabling the network to maintain a continuous memory of its inputs.
     |     *   **Neural Network Layers**:
     |         *   **Input Layer**: Receives data as input.
     |         *   **Hidden Layers**: Processes and transforms the input data through various operations (e.g., convolutional layers, pooling).
     |         *   **Output Layer**: Produces a predicted output based on the transformed data.

# Import necessary libraries
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

# Define the neural network architecture
class Net(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self):
        super(Net, self).__init__()
        self.fc1 = nn.Linear(784, 128)  # Input layer (28x28 images) -> Hidden layer (128 units)
        self.relu = nn.ReLU()
        self.dropout = nn.Dropout(p=0.2)

    def forward(self, x):
        out = self.fc1(x)
        out = self.relu(out)
        out = self.dropout(out)
        return out

# Initialize the model and device
model = Net()
device = torch.device("cuda:0" if torch.cuda.is_available() else "cpu")

# Move the model to the chosen device
model.to(device)

# Set up training parameters
batch_size = 32
learning_rate = 0.001
epochs = 10

# Train the model
for epoch in range(epochs):
    for i, (x, y) in enumerate(train_loader):
        # Move input data to the chosen device
        x, y = x.to(device), y.to(device)

        # Perform forward pass and compute loss
        out = model(x)
        loss = criterion(out, y)

        # Backward pass and update weights
        loss.backward()
        optimizer.step()

    print(f"Epoch {epoch+1}, Loss: {loss.item()}")
```

```spl
-- Recipe 27: Data Extraction
-- Pull structured fields from messy text: names, dates, amounts, references.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained extraction on real-world noisy input.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="Please process payment of USD 4,250.00 to Riverside Consulting (ref: PO-8821) by end of March."
--
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="$(cat invoice.txt)" format="invoice"

CREATE FUNCTION extraction_schema(format TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS JSON AS $$
SELECT CASE format
  WHEN 'invoice' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "invoice_number": {"type": "string"},
      "vendor":         {"type": "string"},
      "amount":         {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string", "default": "USD"},
      "issue_date":     {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "due_date":       {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "line_items":     {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"description": {"type": "string"}, "amount": {"type": "number"}}}},
      "payment_terms":  {"type": "string"},
      "po_reference":   {"type": "string"}
    }
  }'
  WHEN 'contract' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "parties":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "effective_date": {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "expiry_date":    {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "value":          {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string"},
      "jurisdiction":   {"type": "string"},
      "key_obligations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
  ELSE '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "names":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "organizations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "dates":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "amounts":      {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "number"}, "currency": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "references":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "locations":    {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
END
$$;

PROMPT extract_fields
SELECT
    system_role('You are a data extraction specialist. Extract all relevant fields from the text and return valid JSON matching the schema exactly. If a field is not present in the text, omit it. Return only the JSON object.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extraction_schema(context.format) AS schema,
    context.format AS format
GENERATE extract(text, schema)
```

}

```spl
-- Recipe 28: Customer Support Triage
-- Classify → route → draft response in a single workflow.
-- Demonstrates multi-step pipeline with branching, deterministic tool calls for
-- order lookup, and LLM generation grounded in real order data.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="My account has been charged twice for the same order #ORD-12345"
--
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="Package for order ORD-67890 hasn't arrived, it's been 10 days" \
--       product="CloudDash" tone="empathetic"
--
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="I can't log in, password reset email never arrives" \
--       tone="friendly"

-- ── Helper functions (evaluated locally, zero LLM cost) ────────────────────

CREATE FUNCTION support_categories()
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Ticket categories and their routing:
- billing:      finance team, SLA 4h,  high priority
                Covers: duplicate charges, wrong amounts, refund requests, invoice issues
- account:      auth team,    SLA 2h,  high priority
                Covers: login failures, password reset, account locked, 2FA issues
- shipping:     fulfillment,  SLA 6h,  high priority
                Covers: delayed delivery, wrong item received, missing package, tracking problems
- technical:    engineering,  SLA 8h,  medium priority
                Covers: app bugs, API errors, integration failures, data export issues
- product:      product team, SLA 24h, medium priority
                Covers: feature requests, how-to questions, documentation gaps
- complaint:    customer success, SLA 1h, urgent
                Covers: angry customers, escalation threats, repeated unresolved issues
- general:      first-line,   SLA 48h, low priority
                Covers: general inquiries, account queries, other
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION response_tone_guide(tone TEXT DEFAULT 'professional')
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE tone
  WHEN 'empathetic' THEN 'Warm and understanding. Acknowledge frustration. Use "I understand" and "I am sorry". Avoid jargon. Refer to the customer by first name.'
  WHEN 'formal'     THEN 'Professional and precise. Use formal language. Reference ticket numbers and order IDs explicitly. State next steps clearly.'
  WHEN 'friendly'   THEN 'Conversational and positive. Use the customer first name. End with an enthusiastic offer to help further.'
  ELSE                   'Professional but approachable. Concise. Action-oriented. Reference specific order details when available. No excessive apologies.'
END
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION order_context_prompt(order_context TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE
  WHEN order_context = 'No order number found in this ticket.'
    THEN 'No order number was referenced — respond based on the ticket text alone.'
  WHEN order_context LIKE '%not found in system%'
    THEN 'The customer referenced an order number but it was not found. Ask them to confirm the order ID.'
  ELSE
    'Use the following verified order record to ground your response with accurate details (amounts, dates, status):

' || order_context
END
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW support_triage
    INPUT:  @ticket  TEXT,
            @product TEXT DEFAULT 'CloudDash',
            @tone    TEXT DEFAULT 'professional'
    OUTPUT: @drafted_response TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Extract any order numbers mentioned in the ticket (deterministic tool — no LLM)
    CALL extract_order_numbers(@ticket) INTO @order_numbers

    -- Step 2: Fetch the matching order record(s) from orders.json (deterministic tool — no LLM)
    CALL lookup_order(@order_numbers) INTO @order_context

    -- Step 3: Classify the ticket using categories + order data as grounding context
    GENERATE classify_ticket(@ticket, support_categories(), order_context_prompt(@order_context)) INTO @classification_json

    -- Step 4: Extract key structured details from the ticket
    GENERATE extract_ticket_details(@ticket, @order_context) INTO @details_json

    -- Step 5: Score urgency 0-10 based on ticket text and classification
    GENERATE detect_urgency(@ticket, @classification_json) INTO @urgency_score

    -- Step 6: Route — escalate immediately if urgency > 8, otherwise draft response
    EVALUATE @urgency_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            GENERATE escalation_alert(@ticket, @classification_json, @order_context) INTO @alert
            COMMIT @alert WITH status = 'escalated', priority = 'critical'
        OTHERWISE
            -- Step 7: Draft a grounded, tone-appropriate response
            GENERATE draft_response(
                @ticket,
                @classification_json,
                @details_json,
                @order_context,
                @product,
                response_tone_guide(@tone)
            ) INTO @drafted_response

            -- Step 8: Quality-check the draft (0-10); revise once if score < 6
            GENERATE check_response_quality(@drafted_response, @ticket, @order_context) INTO @quality_score

            EVALUATE @quality_score
                WHEN < 6 THEN
                    GENERATE improve_response(@drafted_response, @ticket, @order_context) INTO @drafted_response
                    COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'drafted_revised'
                OTHERWISE
                    COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'drafted', quality = @quality_score
            END
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        GENERATE fallback_response(@ticket, @product) INTO @drafted_response
        COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'fallback'
END
```

```python
class TicketDetailsExtractor:
    def __init__(self):
        self.output = []

    def process_input(self, input1, input2):
        # Step 1: Connect to the database (not applicable in this case)
        pass

        # Step 2: Run the stored procedure
        if input1 == "My account has been charged twice for the same order #12345":
            self.extract_order_numbers(12345)

    def extract_order_numbers(self, order_number):
        if order_number == 12345:
            # Simulating database query to get the data
            orders = [
                {"order_number": 123, "date": "2022-01-01", "time": "12:00", "account_holder": "John Doe"},
                {"order_number": 456, "date": "2022-01-02", "time": "14:00", "account_holder": "Jane Smith"},
                {"order_number": 789, "date": "2022-01-03", "time": "10:00", "account_holder": "Bob Johnson"}
            ]

            # Step 3: Review the output
            for order in orders:
                if order["order_number"] == order_number:
                    self.output.append({
                        "order_number": order["order_number"],
                        "date": order["date"],
                        "time": order["time"],
                        "account_holder": order["account_holder"]
                    })

        elif input1 == "To execute the stored procedure and investigate why an account has been charged twice for the same order number, follow these steps:":
            self.process_input(input2)

    def print_output(self):
        if len(self.output) > 0:
            for detail in self.output:
                print(f"Order Number: {detail['order_number']}")
                print(f"Date: {detail['date']}")
                print(f"Time: {detail['time']}")
                print(f"Account Holder: {detail['account_holder']}")
                print("-"*50)
        else:
            print("No output found.")

# Example usage
extractor = TicketDetailsExtractor()
input1 = "My account has been charged twice for the same order #12345"
input2 = "To execute the stored procedure and investigate why an account has been charged twice for the same order number, follow these steps:"
input3 = "My account has been charged again!"
extractor.process_input(input1, input2)
extractor.print_output()

```sql
DELIMITER //

CREATE PROCEDURE extract_order_numbers()
BEGIN
    SELECT order_number 
    FROM orders 
    GROUP BY order_number 
    HAVING COUNT(*) > 1;
END//

DELIMITER ;
```
     | This procedure groups the orders by order number and selects those with a count greater than 1, which indicates that they have been charged twice.

```sql
DELIMITER //

CREATE PROCEDURE extract_order_numbers()
BEGIN
    SELECT o.order_number, 
           o.date, 
           o.time, 
           a.account_holder 
    FROM orders o 
    JOIN accounts a ON o.account_id = a.id 
    GROUP BY o.order_number 
    HAVING COUNT(*) > 1;
END//

DELIMITER ;
```

    def __init__(self):

     | 
     | class ResponseChecker:
     |     def __init__(self):
     |         self.tasks = []
     | 
     |     def check_response_quality(self, response):
     |         # Extract task details from the response
     |         match = re.search(r'Task Name: (.*)', response)
     |         if match:
     |             self.tasks.append(match.group(1))
     | 
     |         # Check for consistency in task classification
     |         task_classification = re.search(r'Task Type: (.*)', response)
     |         if task_classification and len(self.tasks) > 0:
     |             if task_classification.group(1) != 'Natural Language Processing (NLP) / Sentiment Analysis':
     |                 return False
     | 
     |         # Verify that all tasks are resolved
     |         for i, task in enumerate(self.tasks):
     |             match = re.search(r'Task Number: (\d+)', response)
     |             if match:
     |                 self.tasks[i] = int(match.group(1))
     | 
     |         # Check for consistency in task numbers
     |         if len(set(self.tasks)) != 1:
     |             return False
     | 
     |         # If all checks pass, the response quality is good
     |         return True
     |

```spl
-- Recipe 29: Meeting Notes → Action Items
-- Transcript in, structured TODO list + owners out.
-- Demonstrates multi-step extraction + structured output from long-form text,
-- with deterministic tool calls for file loading, speaker extraction,
-- date normalisation, and ownership validation.
--
-- Usage (load from file):
--   spl run cookbook/29_meeting_actions/meeting_actions.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/29_meeting_actions/tools.py \
--       filename=sprint_planning.txt \
--       output_format=markdown
--
-- Usage (inline transcript):
--   spl run cookbook/29_meeting_actions/meeting_actions.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/29_meeting_actions/tools.py \
--       transcript="Alice: fix the login bug by Friday. Bob: I will handle it." \
--       output_format=json
--
-- Sample transcripts in transcripts/:
--   sprint_planning.txt   Full sprint planning with assignments and decisions
--   design_review.txt     API architecture review with open questions
--   standup.txt           Short daily standup with blockers

-- ── Schema (zero LLM cost — injected as grounding context) ─────────────────

CREATE FUNCTION action_item_schema()
RETURNS JSON AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "meeting_title":   {"type": "string"},
    "meeting_date":    {"type": "string"},
    "attendees":       {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "meeting_summary": {"type": "string"},
    "decisions": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {"type": "string"}
    },
    "action_items": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "task":     {"type": "string"},
          "owner":    {"type": "string", "description": "Person responsible. Use exact speaker name or empty string if unassigned."},
          "due_date": {"type": "string", "description": "Due date or relative phrase e.g. Friday, next Wednesday, end of sprint"},
          "priority": {"type": "string", "enum": ["high", "medium", "low"]},
          "context":  {"type": "string", "description": "Why this task matters or what triggered it"}
        },
        "required": ["task", "priority"]
      }
    },
    "open_questions": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {"type": "string"}
    },
    "next_meeting": {"type": "string"}
  },
  "required": ["meeting_summary", "action_items"]
}
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW meeting_to_actions
    INPUT:  @filename      TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @transcript    TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @output_format TEXT DEFAULT 'json'
    OUTPUT: @output TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Load transcript from file if filename provided (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL load_transcript(@filename) INTO @file_content

    -- Step 2: Extract speaker names for grounding the LLM (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL extract_speakers(@file_content) INTO @speakers_from_file
    CALL extract_speakers(@transcript)   INTO @speakers_from_inline

    -- Step 3: Normalise transcript — merge sources, clean formatting, identify speakers
    GENERATE normalize_transcript(
        @file_content,
        @transcript,
        @speakers_from_file,
        @speakers_from_inline
    ) INTO @clean_transcript

    -- Step 4: Extract structured action items grounded by schema and known speakers
    GENERATE extract_actions(
        @clean_transcript,
        action_item_schema(),
        @speakers_from_file,
        @speakers_from_inline
    ) INTO @structured_json

    -- Step 5: Normalise relative due dates to ISO-8601 (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL normalize_dates(@structured_json) INTO @structured_json

    -- Step 6: Validate ownership — flag unowned high-priority items (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL validate_ownership(@structured_json) INTO @validation_notes

    -- Step 7: Format and commit output
    EVALUATE @output_format
        WHEN = 'markdown' THEN
            GENERATE format_as_markdown(@structured_json, @validation_notes) INTO @output
            COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete', format = 'markdown'
        WHEN = 'email' THEN
            GENERATE format_as_email(@structured_json, @validation_notes) INTO @output
            COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete', format = 'email'
        OTHERWISE
            SET @output = @structured_json
            COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete', format = 'json'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Long transcript: summarise first, then extract from the summary
        GENERATE summarize_transcript(@clean_transcript) INTO @summary
        GENERATE extract_actions(@summary, action_item_schema(), @speakers_from_file, @speakers_from_inline) INTO @structured_json
        CALL normalize_dates(@structured_json) INTO @structured_json
        CALL validate_ownership(@structured_json) INTO @validation_notes
        SET @output = @structured_json
        COMMIT @output WITH status = 'complete_chunked'
END
```

```output
The provided Python code appears to be designed to parse a transcript of a conversation between two speakers, Alice and Bob. The goal is to separate the text spoken by each speaker into individual lines.

However, the current implementation has several issues:

1.  It does not handle cases where a line contains both 'Alice' and 'Bob'. This would result in only one being added to their respective lists.
2.  It does not account for lines that do not start with either "Alice" or "Bob", but still contain the names of the speakers. These would be ignored by the current implementation.
3.  The use of regular expressions (regex) is not fully utilized. Regex can be used to extract only the speaker's name from each line, rather than splitting on a colon.
4.  The code does not include any error handling or input validation.

Here's an improved version of the code that addresses these issues:

```python
import re

def normalize_dates(transcript):
    """
    Extracts lines spoken by Alice and Bob from a transcript.

    Args:
        transcript (str): A multiline string containing the conversation transcript.

    Returns:
        dict: A dictionary with keys 'Alice' and 'Bob', each mapped to a list of lines spoken by that speaker.
    """

    # Initialize empty lists for each speaker's lines
    alice_lines = []
    bob_lines = []

    # Split the transcript into lines
    lines = transcript.split('\n')

    # Iterate over each line in the transcript
    for line in lines:
        # Use regular expression to match the name at the start of the line and extract it
        match = re.match(r'^(Alice|Bob):', line)
        
        if match:
            speaker, _ = match.groups()
            
            if speaker.strip().lower() == 'alice':
                alice_lines.append(line)
            elif speaker.strip().lower() == 'bob':
                bob_lines.append(line)

    # Return a dictionary with the extracted lines
    return {
        'Alice': alice_lines,
        'Bob': bob_lines
    }

# Example usage:
transcript = """
Alice: we need to fix the login bug before Friday.
Bob: I'll handle it.
Alice: also need to update the docs
"""

speakers = normalize_dates(transcript)

for speaker, lines in speakers.items():
    print(f"{speaker}:")
    for line in lines:
        print(line)
```

```python
import re

def extract_speakers(transcript):
    # Initialize empty lists for each speaker's lines
    alice_lines = []
    bob_lines = []

    # Split the transcript into lines
    lines = transcript.split('\n')

    # Iterate over each line in the transcript
    for line in lines:
        # Use regular expression to match the name at the start of the line
        match = re.match(r'^(Alice|Bob):', line)
        
        if match:
            # If it's a match, extract the speaker's name and any remaining text
            speaker, _ = match.groups()
            text = line.strip().lstrip(': ').strip()
            
            if speaker == 'Alice':
                alice_lines.append(text)
            elif speaker == 'Bob':
                bob_lines.append(text)

    return {
        'Alice': alice_lines,
        'Bob': bob_lines
    }

# Example usage:
transcript = """
Alice: we need to fix the login bug before Friday.
Bob: I'll handle it.
Alice: also need to update the docs
"""

speakers = extract_speakers(transcript)

for speaker, lines in speakers.items():
    print(f"{speaker}:")
    for line in lines:
        print(line)
